Pro Private Markets
Cinven Sells CPA Global for 2.4 Billion to Leonard Green

By Ed Ballard 
270 words
29 August 2017
15:56
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Cinven has agreed to sell CPA Global Ltd., a provider of tools for managing intellectual property, to U.S. buyout house Leonard Green & Partners.

The transaction valued CPA at 2.4 billion ($3.1 billion), including debt, a person familiar with the matter said. Cinven paid 950 million for the company in 2012, the person added.

Based in Jersey in the Channel Islands, CPA provides software, data analytics tools and other services that allow corporates and law firms to keep track of their intellectual property. It manages more than two million patents for over 10,000 customers, according to the statement.

Since it bought CPA, Cinven has invested more than 100 million in improving its technology platform, according to the statement. CPA made six add-on acquisitions under Cinven's ownership as the London-based firm sought to expand the business internationally.

"Cinven and CPA Global's entrepreneurial management team have led CPA Global's transition to a technology-led business, by investing in the company's software suite and technology platform, and extending into other parts of the IP process such as patent filing and patent data & analytics," said Anthony Cardona, principal at Cinven.

Leonard Green, based in Los Angeles, closed its seventh buyout fund on $9.6 billion last year. Since it was founded in 1989 the firm has invested in more than 80 companies, according to its website. It typically targets service-oriented companies in sectors including consumer products, health care and retail.

Write to Ed Ballard at "mailto:ed.ballard@wsj.com"

Pro Private Markets
The Roundup

4,905 words
29 August 2017
14:22
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

U.S. & CANADA

Buyouts

Charlesbank Capital Partners, Boston

Charlesbank Capital Partners is seeking $2.75 billion in total commitments for Charlesbank Equity Fund IX LP and up to $700 million for a separate overage fund, according to a document from the Massachusetts Pension Reserves Investment Management Board. If the fund hits the $3.45 billion in aggregate commitments it is seeking, it would surpass the size of the firm's previous fund, which closed at its $1.75 billion hard cap in 2014. Charlesbank typically invests in U.S. companies with enterprise values of $150 million to $1.5 billion.

Falcon Seaboard Resources, Houston

Falcon Seaboard Resources launched Falcon Seaboard Permian Fund I LP, a $145 million fund that will target projects in the Permian Basin. The firm intends to purchase acreage as well as invest and work with exploration and production companies that develop oil and gas leases, pursue drilling projects and acquire established reserves, according to a news release. Falcon Seaboard is a new firm affiliated with energy company Falcon Seaboard Diversified Inc.

Five Point Capital Partners, Houston

Five Point Capital Partners is targeting $650 million for a new fund dedicated to midstream oil and gas investments in the U.S., said people familiar with the matter. The firm, which invests primarily in energy infrastructure, in 2015 announced a final closing of more than $450 million for its maiden pool. That pool comprised Five Point Capital Midstream Fund I LP and Five Point Capital Midstream Fund II LP.

H.I.G. Capital, Miami

H.I.G. Capital launched fundraising for H.I.G Advantage Buyout Fund LP, which is seeking $2.25 billion, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. H.I.G.'s private-equity arm invests in small to midmarket companies valued at $50 million to $500 million.

LightBay Capital, Los Angeles

LightBay Capital is aiming to collect $450 million for its debut fund, said people familiar with the matter. The firm began meeting with investors in April and expects to hold a first closing for the fund in the fall, the people said. LightBay hasn't yet set a cap for the vehicle, the people added. The firm aims to make midmarket buyout and distressed investments.

Monogram Capital Partners, Beverly Hills, Calif.

Consumer-sector investor Monogram Capital Partners is making progress toward the target for its first fund. Monogram Capital Partners I LP is seeking $125 million and held a substantial first closing, said a person with knowledge of the matter. The fund likely will hit its target in the fall, the person said. Monogram focuses on lower midmarket companies with revenue of $5 million to $50 million in the apparel, beauty, pet, consumer health-care, food and restaurant segments.

Novacap, Longueuil, Quebec

Novacap closed Novacap TMT V at the fund's hard cap of 840 million Canadian dollars ($665.8 million) to invest in the technology, media and telecommunications sector. The firm plans to use the vehicle to back companies throughout the U.S. and Canada with annual revenue of C$30 million to C$300 million. Novacap expects to make equity investments of C$20 million to C$100 million. The firm's previous TMT fund closed with C$375 million in 2014.

Oak Hill Capital Partners, New York

Oak Hill Capital Partners wrapped up fundraising for its fourth buyout fund with $2.65 billion. The final tally for Oak Hill Capital Partners IV came in ahead of a $2 billion target and a $2.5 billion hard cap specified in a news release, but short of an initial $3 billion offering amount indicated in 2015 filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Investors in Fund IV include Minnesota State Board of Investment and fund of funds Altegris KKR Commitments Fund. At the time of the closing, the firm had invested more than $900 million of the vehicle across five platforms. Fund IV's closing comes roughly eight years after the midmarket firm raised $3.8 billion for its previous fund. Oak Hill targets control investments of $100 million to $300 million in North American companies across four core sectors: consumer, retail and distribution; industrials; media and communications; and services.

Onex Corp., Toronto

Onex Corp. rounded up at least $5.72 billion so far for its fifth core buyout fund, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The Canadian firm recently disclosed in its quarterly earnings results that it held a first closing of $5.2 billion for Onex Partners V LP in July, including a $2 billion commitment from Onex itself. Investors that committed to the fund include the New Jersey Division of Investment and the Texas County and District Retirement System, according to disclosures by the pension systems. Onex's previous buyout fund wrapped up with $5.66 billion in 2014.

Patriot Financial Partners, Philadelphia

Patriot Financial Partners collected at least $128.2 million so far for Patriot Financial Partners III LP, which has a $300 million goal, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm also was seeking $300 million for its last offering, Private Equity Analyst previously reported. That fund and a parallel vehicle had collected at least $293 million in 2015, though it isn't clear from public records whether that amount represented a final closing. Patriot focuses on noncontrol deals mainly in community banks, thrift institutions and financial-services companies in the U.S.

Southfield Capital, Greenwich, Conn.

Southfield Capital held a final closing for Southfield Capital II LP with capital commitments of about $200 million. The vehicle reached its hard cap and exceeded its $175 million target. Southfield seeks buyouts in the outsourced business-services and finance sectors. The firm targets deals with companies generating $4 million to $12 million in annual earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization.

True Green Capital Management, Westport, Conn.

Energy infrastructure firm True Green Capital Management closed its latest fund, True Green Capital Fund III LP, at its $350 million hard cap. The firm specializes in investing in distributed power generation, with a focus on commercial photovoltaic solar projects. At the time of the closing, the firm had deployed about 30% of the fund's capital. True Green closed on $113 million in 2015 for its previous vehicle, True Green Capital Opportunity Partnership LP.

Union Park Capital, Boston

Union Park Capital is seeking $150 million for Union Park Capital II LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm typically invests $10 million to $50 million in businesses with $10 million to $75 million in annual revenue. Union Park backs companies in North America and Europe that make technology for the industrial sector. The firm in 2013 recorded a goal of nearly $100.4 million for its first fund in an SEC filing. In early 2016, Union Park in a news release said it expanded the debut fund by 40% to $150 million. The firm at the time said its main investors included the University of Texas Investment Management Co.

WM Partners, Fort Lauderdale, Fla.

WM Partners, a firm focused on companies in the health and wellness sector, held a final closing for its first fund. HPH Specialized Fund 1 LP and a parallel vehicle raised more than $307 million in commitments, exceeding a $300 million target. The fund received an anchor commitment from New York firm Vanterra Capital. Principals of WM Partners made an in-kind contribution to the fund in the form of Wellnext Holdings, a Sunrise, Fla.-based nutritional supplements company. The fund is focused on acquiring lower midmarket companies "and integrating them into one consolidated platform," Wellnext, according to a news release. The firm backs companies with annual revenue of $20 million to $60 million.

Yellow Wood Partners, Boston

Yellow Wood Partners closed on commitments of $370 million for Yellow Wood Capital Partners II LP. The firm plans to back four to five platform investments through its new fund, with each potentially sealing two to four add-on acquisitions, said Dana Schmaltz, founding partner of Yellow Wood. The firm intends to invest at least $50 million per portfolio company and could deploy more than $150 million. Yellow Wood announced a $225 million closing for its first pool of capital in 2012.

Distressed/Turnaround

Benefit Street Partners, New York

Benefit Street Partners, the credit-investment unit of Providence Equity Partners, closed a special-situations fund at $750 million, said a person familiar with the matter. The fund, Benefit Street Partners Special Situations Fund LP, was oversubscribed and closed at its hard cap, the person said. Among investors in the fund is the New Jersey Division of Investment, according to a pension document. The firm manages private debt, liquid loans, high-yield debt, special situations and other credit strategies. The special-situations group aims to invest in stressed or distressed companies in the upper middle market.

Castlelake, Minneapolis

After four months on the fundraising trail, Castlelake closed its fifth general-purpose fund, Castlelake V LP, at the vehicle's hard cap of $2.4 billion in capital commitments. Formed in 2005 with backing from TPG, Castlelake focuses on investments in alternative assets, subperforming loans, dislocated industries, corporate special situations and aircraft. The firm manages private funds and debt vehicles with more than $10 billion in assets. The new fund's predecessor, Castlelake IV LP, closed in 2015 with $1.9 billion in capital commitments.

Funds of Funds

Private Advisors, Richmond, Va.

Private Advisors closed its seventh private-equity fund of funds focused on investments in small North American companies. Private Advisors Small Co Private Equity Fund VII LP closed at its $350 million hard cap. The vehicle invests in lower midmarket buyout, growth and distressed private-equity funds that typically are less than $750 million in size. The fund also makes equity co-investments and secondary investments. Private Advisors is an affiliate of New York Life Investment Management LLC, which is a subsidiary of New York Life Insurance Co.

RCP Advisors, Chicago

Fund-of-funds manager RCP Advisors raised at least $132 million so far for its latest vehicle, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. RCP Fund XII LP is targeting $250 million, according to the filing, and has a total offering amount of $400 million. RCP focuses exclusively on North American buyouts. The firm's funds of funds typically back lower midmarket buyout managers with $200 million to $1 billion in committed capital. RCP also is a secondary purchaser of limited partner interests and makes co-investments.

Mezzanine & Other Debt

CapitalSpring, New York

Restaurant investor CapitalSpring gathered commitments of $725 million for its fifth investment fund. The amount, which spans CapitalSpring Investment Partners V LP and parallel funds, exceeded a $500 million target. Investors that committed to the fund include the Rhode Island State Investment Commission, Ohio Police & Fire Pension Fund and San Antonio Fire and Police Pension Fund, according to pension documents and meeting notes. CapitalSpring held an initial closing for Fund V in 2016 and, when it announced the fund's final closing, had made seven investments from the vehicle. The firm invests $10 million to $100 million in restaurants in the form of structured loans and private equity.

Napier Park Global Capital, New York

Napier Park Global Capital and publicly traded Air Lease Corp. have established a new fund to invest in commercial aircraft and lease them to airlines. Blackbird Capital II LLC so far has sealed $231 million of equity commitments and is able to collect additional pledges in the next 18 months. Blackbird Capital II will acquire aircraft using equity and debt financing. Air Lease and Napier Park closed their first Blackbird fund in 2015 with $500 million in total equity commitments, with a goal of acquiring about $2 billion of commercial aircraft assets.

O'Brien-Staley Partners, Edina, Minn.

O'Brien-Staley Partners is weighing a plan to raise $500 million for credit plays, in what would be the firm's first impact investment fund, said people familiar with the matter. Through OSP Fixed Income Impact Fund LP, the credit firm expects to make loans and invest in credits that are secured against assets, said one of the people. At the same time, O'Brien-Staley will aim to meet or exceed goals on objectives such as the jobs it can create and the buildings it can make greener.

Orion Energy Partners, New York

Orion Energy Partners raised about $650 million so far for its first energy-infrastructure fund and expects to close the vehicle with $750 million to $800 million, said a person familiar with the matter. The firm, which set a $750 million target for Orion Energy Credit Opportunities Fund II LP, invested nearly 40% of that amount across five deals, the person said. The firm is expected to wrap up the vehicle by the end of September. Orion was formed in 2015 by co-founders including Nazar Massouh, who is the firm's chief executive. Mr. Massouh previously was an executive at Energy Capital Partners, where he managed an $805 million fund called Energy Capital Partners Mezzanine Opportunities Fund LP. Orion's direct-lending vehicle is designated Fund II although it is the firm's first fund because of the vehicle's similarity to that pool, said the person. Orion targets debt investments of $25 million to $200 million in midmarket energy-infrastructure businesses.

Thoma Bravo, Chicago

Thoma Bravo aims to raise $750 million for Thoma Bravo Credit Fund I LP and related vehicles, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm makes equity investments in the software and technology-enabled services sectors of at least $100 million and can access co-investment funds for deals of $1 billion or more. Thoma Bravo focuses on U.S. businesses that record more than $20 million of annual earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization.

Twin Brook Capital Partners, Chicago

Twin Brook Capital Partners, the midmarket direct-lending subsidiary of Angelo Gordon & Co., raised more than $2.3 billion across its second fund, AG Direct Lending Fund II LP, and separately managed accounts. The fund itself collected $1.6 billion, beating a $1 billion target. The pool's predecessor closed last year and raised $850 million, including commitments to a fund and separate accounts. Fund II will manage a portfolio of midmarket floating rate, senior secured loans with a focus on first-lien secured debt. It will target senior financing transactions of up to $200 million.

Wafra Capital Partners, New York

Commercial aviation company GA Telesis LLC and Wafra Capital Partners raised $500 million to establish their second fund focused on aviation deals. The vehicle, which will be named GA Telesis Aviation Investments 2 LLC, will seek to invest in assets such as commercial aircraft, engines and airport-infrastructure equipment, for long-term or short-term leases and financing. The vehicle will have an investment capacity of up to $2 billion using its commitments, GA Telesis's own capital and existing credit facilities. GA Telesis and Wafra announced the launch of the vehicle's predecessor, which also collected $500 million, in 2014.

Secondary

Portfolio Advisors, Darien, Conn.

Portfolio Advisors gathered at least $629.7 million so far for Portfolio Advisors Secondary Fund III LP and at least $206.3 million for an affiliated offshore vehicle, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. Private Equity Analyst reported in 2015 the firm was targeting $1 billion for the offering to buy private-equity fund stakes on the secondary market. The filings don't indicate a target or hard cap. Portfolio Advisors announced the closing of its previous secondary fund with commitments of $910 million in 2014.

Venture Capital

AI Fund LP, Palo Alto, Calif.

AI Fund LP is aiming to raise $150 million, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The fund didn't record any commitments in the filing. Andrew Ng, co-founder of online education company Coursera Inc. and former chief scientist at Baidu Inc., is listed in the filing as the person responsible for the fund.

Aquiline Capital Partners, New York

Aquiline Capital Partners completed raising capital for a new fund that aims to apply the firm's focus on financial services to smaller, expanding companies. Aquiline Technology Growth Fund LP closed at about $190 million, exceeding its $150 million target, to invest in companies focused on insurance and the broader financial-services sector. The fund invests $3 million to $15 million in companies raising funding in Series A rounds through the growth-equity stage. Aquiline typically targets midmarket companies.

Canaan Partners, Menlo Park, Calif.

Canaan Partners collected $800 million for investments in technology and health-care startups. Canaan XI LP represents the firm's largest fund, surpassing the $675 million Canaan collected for its 10th fund in 2014. The firm was formed as a spinoff of General Electric Co. in 1987.

Harbert Management Corp., Birmingham, Ala.

Harbert Management Corp. held a final closing for Harbert Growth Partners IV LP at $120 million. The fund invests in emerging growth-stage technology and health-care companies. A previous vehicle, Harbert Venture Partners III LP, had total equity commitments of about $78 million.

K9 Ventures, Palo Alto, Calif.

K9 Ventures raised $42 million for its third technology-focused preseed fund. Manu Kumar, the firm's founder, said he made two investments a year in 2015 and 2016, but is targeting three to four a year in the new fund. The firm aims to provide the first institutional money invested into a company. K9's second fund secured $40 million and closed in 2012.

LiveOak Venture Partners, Austin, Texas

LiveOak Venture Partners plans to raise $110 million for LiveOak Venture Partners II LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm didn't record any capital commitments to the vehicle in the filing. LiveOak's inaugural fund closed with $109 million in 2014. The firm targets information-technology and technology-enabled services deals in Texas, with initial transactions of $1 million to $4 million and total investments of $7 million to $10 million.

Primary Venture Partners, New York

Primary Venture Partners is raising as much as $100 million for its second fund, said a person familiar with the situation. The new fund was targeting $80 million but likely will close on more than that, the person said. Primary Venture, which closed a $60 million pool in 2015, will continue making seed investments in New York- and Northeast-based commerce and enterprise-software startups, the person said. The firm previously operated as High Peaks Venture Partners.

Round13 Capital, Toronto

Round13 Capital raised $95 million Canadian dollars ($747.9 million) for a new fund. The firm plans to invest up to $5 million in Canadian startups for sales and marketing efforts.

TenOneTen Ventures, Los Angeles

TenOneTen Ventures is looking to raise $50 million for its latest fund, TenOneTen Ventures II LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm didn't disclose any commitments to the fund in the filing. TenOneTen's first fund closed in 2015 at nearly $18.2 million, said Managing Partner David Waxman. Mr. Waxman previously said the firm concentrates on seed- and early-stage technology companies in the Los Angeles area, but is open to investing in other parts of the country and in later-stage companies.

EUROPE

Buyouts

ArchiMed, Lyon, France

ArchiMed is returning to market seeking to raise its second fund, said people familiar with the matter. The firm is aiming to collect  ¬200 million ($233.4 million) or more, one of the people said. ArchiMed's first fund, Med 1, closed with  ¬150 million in 2015. The firm was founded in 2014 to do health-care deals in small and midsize European companies.

C5 Capital, London

C5 Capital, which invests through both private-equity and venture-capital strategies, plans to return to market with a third fund before the end of 2017, said people familiar with the matter. The firm is expected to target about $200 million for the vehicle. The firm also soon will "announce the launch of C5 Partners II," according to its website. That fund will focus on cloud computing companies in Europe, the Middle East and Africa. The firm's first fund, C5 Cyber Partners, focused on cybersecurity and data-analytics companies in the EMEA region.

Carlyle Group LP, Washington

Carlyle Group LP plans to raise  ¬5 billion ($5.83 billion) for its fifth Europe-focused buyout fund, said a person familiar with the matter. The fund is expected to be a third larger than Carlyle's previous European vehicle, Carlyle Europe Partners IV LP, which the firm closed with  ¬3.75 billion in 2015. That fund conducts leveraged buyout transactions throughout the continent.

Exponent Private Equity, London

Exponent Private Equity is targeting 1.5 billion ($1.97 billion) for its latest fund, said a person familiar with the plans. The firm invests in companies in the U.K. and Ireland with enterprise values of 100 million to 400 million. Exponent's third fund, Exponent Private Equity Partners III LP, closed with 1 billion in 2013.

Graphite Capital, London

Graphite Capital will seek more than 500 million ($656.8 million) for a new flagship fund, said an investor briefed on the plans. The midmarket firm primarily seeks buyout deals for U.K. businesses valued at up to 200 million. Its previous fund, Graphite Capital Partners VIII, wrapped up in 2013 with 500 million, according to the firm's website.

Limerston Capital, London

Limerston Capital, a firm focused on the U.K. lower middle market, closed its inaugural fund on more than 200 million ($264.4 million). The firm didn't say exactly how much it raised for the vehicle. Limerston originally sought to raise as much as 300 million for Limerston Capital Partners I LP, Private Equity Analyst sister publication Private Equity News reported in January 2016. The firm will seek controlling stakes in companies with earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of 4 million to 10 million.

Lyceum Capital, London

Lyceum Capital is expected to come back to market this year targeting 350 million ($459.8 million) for a new fund, said investors briefed on the plans. The firm invests 10 million to 75 million in companies in the business-to-business services and technology sectors. To date, it primarily has invested in U.K. businesses. Its most recent fund, Lyceum Capital Partners III, closed with 330 million in 2013.

Mobeus Equity Partners, London

Lower midmarket firm Mobeus Equity Partners beat its 150 million ($195.7 million) target for its debut institutional fund and held a 166 million final closing. Mobeus Equity Partners IV LP will invest 5 million to 15 million initially in U.K. buyout deals. Mobeus, which also manages venture-capital trusts, launched the new fund last year following changes to venture-capital trust legislation in the U.K.

PAI Partners, Paris

PAI Partners is expected to return to market with a  ¬4 billion ($4.66 billion) European fund later this year, said people familiar with the matter. One of the people said the buyout firm could kick off fundraising in the fourth quarter. The fund is expected to be larger than the firm's sixth vehicle, PAI Europe VI, which wrapped up with  ¬3.3 billion in 2015. 

Partners Group, Baar, Switzerland

Partners Group raised  ¬6 billion ($7 billion) for its latest direct private-equity fund. The fund's total is three times larger than the firm's original  ¬2 billion target and four times larger than its  ¬1.5 billion predecessor, which closed in 2014. Partners Group raised  ¬3 billion for the new fund through its flagship program, Partners Group Direct Equity 2016, as well as  ¬3 billion of additional capital. The firm will invest the fund in midmarket and large-cap companies.

Phoenix Equity Partners, London

U.K. midmarket firm Phoenix Equity Partners held a 415 million ($539.3 million) final closing for its fourth fund, Phoenix Equity Partners 2016. Phoenix began marketing the fund in November 2015 with a 500 million target, Private Equity Analyst sister publication Private Equity News previously reported. The firm's previous fund closed on 450 million in 2010, Private Equity Analyst reported at the time.

Summit Partners, Boston

Summit Partners held a final closing for its second European growth-equity fund on the vehicle's  ¬700 million ($809.3 million) hard cap. The fund, Summit Partners Europe Growth Equity Fund II, will target minority and majority equity investments of  ¬20 million to  ¬60 million. In addition to its growth-equity strategy, Summit oversees fixed income and public-equity investments.

Mezzanine & Other Debt

BlueBay Asset Management, London

BlueBay Asset Management raised more than  ¬3 billion ($3.5 billion) for its third private-debt vehicle. Senior Loan Fund I will provide financing for leveraged buyouts, mergers, acquisitions, corporate financing, growth financing and refinancings. When it closed, the fund had completed six deals and was 12% deployed.

Secondary

Glendower Capital, London

Deutsche Bank AG's unit for trading secondhand private-equity fund stakes plans to go it alone and raise a new capital pool. The spinout, Glendower Capital, will continue to manage Deutsche Bank's existing secondary funds and plans to pursue what would be the team's fourth capital pool, said the person. The fund is expected to target about $2 billion. Deutsche Bank will continue to own the portfolio of secondary assets in the bank's first three secondary funds, and Glendower will assume the role of adviser to the funds, said two people familiar with the matter. The third fund in the series closed on $1.66 billion in 2014.

Intermediate Capital Group, London

Intermediate Capital Group closed its latest secondary fund at $1.1 billion. ICG Strategic Secondaries Fund II LP exceeded its $1 billion target. The vehicle is the first blind-pool fund for ICG's secondary group. The fund focuses on private-equity fund restructurings and mature funds. Fund II invests $100 million to $200 million per deal in North American and European businesses, aiming to work with private-equity managers on funds that are seven or more years old, according to a September 2016 ICG investor presentation.

Venture Capital

Abingworth, London

Life-sciences investor Abingworth held a first closing on $220 million for Abingworth Bioventures VII LP, said a person familiar with the matter. The vehicle has a $300 million target and a $400 million hard cap, the person added. The firm, which began fundraising and held an initial dry closing for the fund last year, is expected to hold a final closing near the end of this year, the person said. The previous fund in the series closed in 2014 with 225 million. Abingworth makes venture investments as well as backs small public companies and participates in clinical co-development deals. The firm targets health-care and life-sciences companies in the U.S. and Europe. It generally commits $15 million to $30 million per company, taking either majority or minority stakes.

Idinvest Partners, Paris

Idinvest Partners is in talks with investors to raise a  ¬250 million ($294.5 million) fund to invest in digital and technology businesses throughout Europe, said Benoist Grossmann, a managing partner at the firm. If it hits its target, the fund will be more than 40% larger than its predecessor, Idinvest Digital II, which closed on  ¬140 million in 2015. Idinvest's digital strategy focuses on venture-capital investments. Alongside the digital strategy, Idinvest is aiming to raise about  ¬1.1 billion across two private-debt funds. One fund, which will issue unitranche and mezzanine loans to lower midmarket companies, is targeting  ¬700 million. Idinvest is seeking to raise about  ¬400 million for a senior-debt strategy as well, Private Equity Analyst previously reported. Additionally, the firm is marketing its second growth fund to investors. The vehicle held a  ¬250 million first closing in 2016 and is set to close on its  ¬350 million target by December, Mr. Grossmann said.

Rewired, Lausanne, Switzerland

A team of investors launched Rewired, a venture studio focused on robotics-related startups, with $100 million to invest. The firm's portfolio companies include Open Bionics Ltd., a robotic-limbs specialist in the U.K., and U.S. drone-analytics startup Raptor Maps Inc.

Zouk Capital, London

Zouk Capital is raising its third growth-capital fund in the low-carbon technology space, said people familiar with the matter. The firm will target  ¬300 million ($354.6 million) for the vehicle. In addition to technology-focused growth funds, Zouk manages a strategy that makes European infrastructure investments.

MIDDLE EAST

Venture Capital

Maniv Mobility, Tel Aviv

Maniv Mobility, an early-stage venture fund focused on the auto-technology sector, closed its first fund with $42 million. The firm invests primarily at the seed and Series A stages, and has a portfolio of 15 auto-technology startups, mostly in Israel. It plans to invest in 20 to 25 companies total, said Maniv founder Michael Granoff. Maniv counts among its limited partners InMotion Ventures, Jaguar Land Rover's venture arm; equity crowdfunding company OurCrowd; and French automotive supplier Valeo SA.

StageOne Ventures, Herzliya Pituach, Israel

StageOne Ventures is seeking $110 million for Stage One Venture Capital Fund III LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm didn't record any capital commitments to the vehicle in the filing. StageOne invests in information-technology infrastructure startups.

Deng's Take: Chinese Bank Takes Gamble on Debt

Hurricane Harvey Leaves Congress With a Pair of Financial Challenges

Regulators, Auto Lenders Dig Into Customer-Refunds Process

Australia's Biggest Bank Faces Ethics Check

Critics of Troubled Insurer Dogged by Mysterious Approaches

Chinese Bank Takes Gamble on Debt

BEIJING As the level of corporate debt soars in China, one state-owned bank is scoring some political capital by taking on Beijing's problem in a bigger way than anyone else.

Under a loan-relief program that Beijing started last year, China Construction Bank, the country's second-largest lender by assets, has signed 544.2 billion yuan (about $82 billion) in debt-for-equity swap deals as of last month. That represents more than half of the 1 trillion yuan total that the National Development and Reform Commission says lenders and borrowers have signed so far.

The agreements allow indebted companies to receive debt forgiveness. In exchange, lenders get equity in those businesses. Other state lenders are dabbling in debt-for-equity swaps, but CCB's participation dwarfs those efforts.

The challenge the bank is taking on is huge. Its success will depend in large part on its ability to attract capital from outside investors to help buy loans or invest in converted equity. Analysts expect that in some cases, the bank will contribute only some funds toward the deals, while other parties contribute the majority.

Few outside investors have shown interest in contributing capital so far. Fitch Ratings estimates that CCB has raised funding for only about 10% of the deals it has announced. "Banks' capital constraints will become the main limit on the scale of the scheme, if these entities now start to fund most debt-for-equity deals in this way," the ratings firm said in an analyst report.

If CCB's gamble goes wrong, it could be saddled with near-worthless equity. The bank has set up a new subsidiary to execute the swaps and hold the equity, distancing itself from risks, although it is likely that investors will assume an implicit guarantee from the parent company.

Analysts also say banks are venturing outside their field of expertise by getting further involved in indebted businesses that need complicated restructuring help. CCB has poured 12 billion yuan into its subsidiary. Whether this subsidiary will have the know-how and resources to take on complicated projects has yet to be seen.

For now, the upside for CCB is more political.

More than pursuing a solid business opportunity, analysts say CCB is likely trying to align itself with what Beijing wants. The majority of signed agreements so far are in coal and steel, meaning the debt-for-equity program is helping to keep alive businesses that are strategically important to the government.

According to people familiar with the matter, Beijing hopes that CCB can set up a model other banks can follow. The bank has a lot of state-owned clients, making it an obvious candidate to give the program some momentum.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Hurricane Harvey Leaves Congress With a Pair of Financial Challenges

By Andrew Ackerman, Kristina Peterson and Rachel Witkowski

WASHINGTON Tropical Storm Harvey leaves Congress with a pair of unexpected financial challenges: further shoring up a heavily indebted federal flood insurance program and providing emergency funding for uncovered damage.

Lawmakers will be under pressure when they return to Washington next week to extend the National Flood Insurance Program after the flooding disaster crippled coastal Texas and Louisiana.

The insurance program, created about 50 years ago because private insurers were unwilling to risk catastrophic flood losses, is scheduled to expire Sept. 30. It has a mere $1.7 billion to pay claims and $5.8 billion left that it can borrow from the Treasury, according to the Federal Emergency Management Agency, which manages the program.

Congress likely will have to raise the program's borrowing authority should claims exceed its current cap, these people said.

The contentious flood program went through 17 temporary extensions and four lapses during legislative disputes from 2008 to 2012.

Another issue lawmakers face is emergency aid to address damage that isn't insured, a process that has been controversial on Capitol Hill in recent years. Many conservative Republicans opposed federal aid after superstorm Sandy slammed the East Coast in 2012.

In January 2013, Congress approved a $50.5 billion package to help states rebuild after Sandy, as well as a $9.7 billion increase in the borrowing authority of the flood insurance program.

President Donald Trump said Monday he expects Congress will quickly approve funds to help Texas recover. "As you know, it's going to be a very expensive situation," he told reporters in the Oval Office.

Aides from both parties said it was too early to know how much federal assistance would be needed for Harvey recovery. But already, tension among Republicans was apparent, as GOP lawmakers who fought for aid after Sandy noted that many Texas Republicans had opposed it.

Among those opposing Sandy relief were then-Rep. Mick Mulvaney (R., S.C.), now Mr. Trump's budget director, who said the funds should have been offset by budget trims elsewhere. Only nine Republicans voted for the main Sandy aid in the Senate. Its opponents included both GOP senators from Texas: John Cornyn and Ted Cruz.

GOP Rep. Peter King of New York said Saturday on Twitter: "Ted Cruz & Texas cohorts voted vs NY/NJ aid after Sandy but I'll vote 4 Harvey aid. NY wont abandon Texas. 1 bad turn doesnt deserve another." Mr. King added: "As lifelong NYer w/ NY values I will vote for emergency Harvey $ for Ted Cruz's constituents."

Mr. Cruz said Monday on CNBC that he opposed the Sandy aid bill because he thought it included spending not tied closely enough to relief.

"I didn't think it was appropriate to engage in pork-barrel spending where two-thirds of that bill was unrelated spending that had nothing to do with Sandy," Mr. Cruz said.

Mr. Cornyn objected to the final bill for similar reasons, his spokesman said Monday, noting he has backed other relief after natural disasters.

AshLee Strong, a spokeswoman for House Speaker Paul Ryan (R., Wis.), said Congress "will help those affected by this terrible disaster," adding that the first step is a formal request for resources from the Trump administration.

Democrats have traditionally supported emergency funding without cutting the budget by comparable amounts elsewhere.

"Republicans must be ready to join Democrats in passing a timely relief bill that makes all necessary resources available through emergency spending," House Minority Leader Nancy Pelosi (D., Calif.) said in a statement Monday.

"With the National Flood Insurance Program expiring at the end of September, House Republicans must also move swiftly to ensure that affordable flood insurance continues to be available," she said.

What makes the debate over the insurance program challenging is that the program already has a debt of roughly $25 billion from earlier weather disasters. Much of that stems from Sandy and Hurricane Katrina in 2005. Sandy alone cost $8.4 billion initially, according to FEMA.

Republican Rep. Jeb Hensarling of Texas, who heads the powerful House Financial Services Committee, said the "urgency of the situation" in his home state should put pressure on lawmakers to make sweeping reforms to the flood program.

"A long-term reauthorization is within our means and capacity," he said in an interview Monday. "We have an opportunity to open up this marketplace to competition and to make it more affordable."

Yet lawmakers from both chambers will only be in session at the same time for 12 days in September and already face a host of thorny fiscal deadlines. Congress must act to keep the government running after its funding expires Oct. 1, raise the government's borrowing limit and authorize new funding for children's health insurance, all at a time of flaring tensions between President Donald Trump and congressional Republicans.

The limited time frame is expected to complicate lawmakers' ability to find consensus on broad changes to the flood program, congressional aides said.

While lawmakers agree the program's ills need to be addressed, they have been at odds over how to stabilize it.

Protracted fights in the past led to the temporary lapses in the program, causing thousands of home sales to stall, amid disagreements over whether to change how the program works, such as shifting more of the risk to the private sector from taxpayers.

Regulators, Auto Lenders Dig Into Customer-Refunds Process

By Emily Glazer and AnnaMaria Andriotis

Regulators are reviewing some auto-lending procedures, including those related to borrower refunds, at several banks and other financing businesses in the wake of problems at Wells Fargo & Co., according to people familiar with the matter.

The Consumer Financial Protection Bureau and the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency are examining guaranteed asset protection products and the refund processes associated with them at these firms, the people said.

The CFPB is focusing on the refunding process, these people added. It isn't clear whether the regulatory reviews will lead to any action.

Wells Fargo previously had disclosed that during an internal review it discovered issues relating to the administration of so-called GAP products, particularly customer refunds.

The regulatory reviews are happening as many lenders themselves also check up on their internal controls around the GAP products and related refunds.

Capital One Financial Corp. , Santander Consumer USA Holdings Inc., and U.S. Bancorp are among banks that are reviewing their GAP product refund policies given the situation at Wells Fargo, according to people familiar with the matter. While reviews occur on an ongoing basis, the banks are taking a closer look at procedures meant to ensure borrowers who are entitled to refunds are receiving them, the people said.

GM Financial, Nissan Motor Acceptance Corp., Infiniti Financial Services and Ford Motor Credit Co. are among so-called captive-finance companies checking their internal controls and refunding processes as well, people familiar with the firms said. It isn't clear whether the firms will make changes or what those will be.

GAP coverage is typically sold to consumers through car dealerships. The products offer consumers additional protection beyond standard auto insurance policies and are often financed as part of the customer's loan.

For instance, if a customer has an accident and the vehicle is totaled, so-called GAP waivers could help pay off the loan balance that isn't covered by the customer's primary insurance.

Laws in nine states require that customers receive refunds for unused coverage periods and the holder of the contract the lender often ensures this is done properly.

Some lenders use a third-party provider to oversee the refunding process. Some of those providers, including World Omni Financial Corp., have received questions from lenders following news of Wells Fargo's problems, people familiar with the matter said.

Kenneth Rojc, a lawyer at Chicago-based Nisen & Elliott LLC who manages the law firm's automotive-finance group, said some of his clients are examining whether they have the right procedures in place to implement GAP refunds. That could include how the process works, who provides the refund and whether that firm is doing so in a timely manner, he added.

"Every single one of our auto-finance managers here are saying  What are we doing? Is this something we should be concerned about?'" Mr. Rojc said.

Auto lenders have reason to be cautious. After Wells Fargo cited the problems in a recent quarterly securities filing, The Wall Street Journal and other media reported the San Francisco Federal Reserve made an inquiry about control breakdowns related to GAP refunds. The OCC is also probing Wells Fargo's practices.

Wells Fargo spokeswoman Catherine Pulley said in a statement at the time: "During an internal review, we discovered issues related to a lack of oversight and controls surrounding the administration of guaranteed asset protection &products." Ms. Pulley added the bank is working to make the customer refund process "more consistent for customers."

Australia's Biggest Bank Faces Ethics Check

By Robb M. Stewart

MELBOURNE, Australia  Commonwealth Bank of Australia Ltd. faces a broad ethics health check by regulators, as the fallout over alleged money-laundering compliance breaches expanded.

In an unusual step, the Australian Prudential Regulation Authority on Monday said that it would establish an independent inquiry into the nation's biggest bank, focusing on governance, culture and accountability. The move follows a civil suit brought against the bank by the country's financial-intelligence agency and a review by the securities regulator into compliance by the board and senior executives.

Following a string of scandals, Commonwealth Bank has been a focal point for calls by opposition politicians for a judicial review of the banking industry, a move Prime Minister Malcolm Turnbull's government has deflected with annual parliamentary quizzing of bank executives and plans for a new consumer complaints authority and an accountability regimen for executives.

Commonwealth Bank was among several financial institutions in the country to have faced claims of offering poor financial advice in recent years and has also been accused of failing to honor insurance claims. This month, the Australian Transaction Reports and Analysis Centre filed a federal court suit alleging the bank contravened the Anti-Money Laundering and Counter-Terrorism Financing Act more than 53,700 times, each carrying a maximum penalty of 18 million Australian dollars (US$14.3 million).

The goal of the inquiry is to identify possible organizational and cultural shortcomings that may have led to scandals that have shaken public confidence, said Wayne Byres, chairman of the prudential regulator.

"The Australian community's trust in the banking system has been damaged in recent years, and CBA in particular has been negatively impacted by a number of issues that have affected the reputation of the bank," he said. "Given its position in the Australian financial system, it is critical that community trust is strengthened."

The inquiry has Commonwealth Bank's full support, Chief Executive Ian Narev said. "Over the last few years there have [been] a number of highly publicized issues that have hurt public perception. We've been in the news for all the wrong reasons," he said.

The inquiry panel will take about six months to produce a report that will be made public, the regulator said. Members of the panel have yet to be finalized, but the cost of the review will fall on the bank.

The inquiry wouldn't specifically address matters that overlap with legal proceedings or reviews by other regulators.

"Australia's banks are well-capitalized, well-regulated and financially sound. However, there have been too many cases and events that have damaged their reputation and standing in the eyes of many Australians that warrants our regulators taking action now," Australia Treasurer Scott Morrison said. Mr. Morrison recently met with Commonwealth Bank Chairman Catherine Livingstone in the wake of accusations made by the Australian Transaction Reports and Analysis Centre, or Austrac, and made it clear the government would be considering all options to address the matter.

Days after Austrac's case was filed, Mr. Narev said he would step down by mid-2018. The CEO and senior bank executives lost out on short-term bonuses for the last year through June in the wake of the allegations.

Austrac's allegations center on the use of the bank's "intelligent deposit machines," which allow anonymous deposits of up to A$20,000 in cash at a time to be automatically credited to accounts. Austrac's allegations largely relate to a failure to provide timely reports on transactions above a A$10,000 threshold for over three years from 2012, as well as accusations it failed to report suspicious transactions on time or at all, and of not monitoring customers even after it became aware of suspected money laundering.

The bank has yet to file a response in court but has said a coding error introduced with a software upgrade accounted for the vast majority of reporting failures alleged by the agency.

With the lawsuit, the Australian Securities and Investments Commission has begun its own inquiries to determine whether the bank's directors and executives complied with their duties and if the bank had met licensing and disclosure obligations.

Critics of Troubled Insurer Dogged by Mysterious Approaches

By Mark Maremont

Critics of AmTrust Financial Services Inc., which is being probed by a regulator and pressured by investors, say they have been approached by a series of purported overseas consultants dangling enticing offers. Afterwards, the critics discover these people aren't who they say they are.

Chris Irons, an analyst at research firm GeoInvesting LLC, which has published several reports critical of AmTrust's accounting practices, said he was contacted in July by a woman who identified herself as a London-based consultant to a European software multimillionaire seeking contributors to a new investment website. He agreed to meet at a Philadelphia-area restaurant.

At the dinner, Mr. Irons said, the woman, whom he described as gorgeous, plied him with drinks and slipped in several questions about critiques of AmTrust and its accounting methods. "It was the second or third follow-up question on AmTrust that gave me a lot of pause," he said, adding that she "laughed at many things I said that probably weren't that funny."

But the name the woman gave  Diana Ilic  appears to be a pseudonym. The email address she used with Mr. Irons links to a domain name established a few days before the meeting. The London address for her consulting firm turns out to be a mailbox drop.

A woman who made initial contact on social media on behalf of the purported Ms. Ilic doesn't appear to be who she said she was either. The social-media pages under her name used photos of another woman, a French retail marketer. The purported Ms. Ilic didn't return phone calls or emails to the contacts provided to Mr. Irons.

AmTrust, a fast-growing, New York-based insurance company with $5.5 billion in 2016 revenue, in recent years has attracted skepticism about its results from investors betting against its stock, which has nearly halved this year. The Wall Street Journal, citing people familiar with the matter, reported this year that the Securities and Exchange Commission was investigating AmTrust's accounting practices.

Other AmTrust critics described similar odd approaches to The Wall Street Journal, including an investor who is betting against AmTrust's stock; a journalist who has published articles critical of AmTrust's founders; and Mr. Irons' boss, who said he'd met two months earlier with a different "consultant" dangling a lucrative offer, who then brought up AmTrust.

That consultant, the GeoInvesting team later found, used the same London mail-box drop as Ms. Ilic.

Battles between companies and short sellers sometimes turn nasty and both sides in such disputes occasionally have used private investigators to dig up information, usually in a legitimate fashion. The investigators often are hired through law firms and the information sometimes is used in litigation.

An AmTrust spokeswoman said the company didn't employ investigators to probe its critics. It declined to say whether its lawyers or others in its service had done so. The company has declined to comment on the SEC probe.

AmTrust this year restated some results with relatively minor changes after switching outside auditors.

Investigators using fake identities and misrepresentations could run afoul of several state and federal laws, said Gavin P. Lentz, a Philadelphia attorney and former prosecutor, who isn't involved in the matter. A company that hires such investigators potentially could be held civilly liable, Mr. Lentz said, because these are agents acting on their behalf.

"Generally speaking, as a private investigator you can't misrepresent yourself" in the U.S., said James Cesarano, vice president of ethics and compliance at Kroll Associates Inc., a corporate investigations firm.

In 2016, AmTrust said it hired private Israeli investigators claiming to be consultants to secretly tape a former Italian business partner in Italy, according to a lawsuit AmTrust filed last year against the former partner.

The parties settled after the tape allegedly revealed the former partner had offered a bribe to an arbitrator. Both parties said the settlement was confidential and the former business partner said in a text message that he didn't "corrupt anyone."

AmTrust has been in a long-running battle with short sellers investors who bet against its stock and other critics, who have claimed the insurer burnishes its financials partly by underestimating future claims and through reinsurance transactions with overseas affiliates that had the effect of hiding losses.

AmTrust, which denies such allegations, several years ago filed a legal notice threatening to sue GeoInvesting and a vocal short seller, Casey Nelson. AmTrust also has attacked short sellers several times on investor conference calls. On a May call, CEO Barry Zyskind called short seller claims "false" and "baseless."

The ostensible consultants contacting AmTrust critics have legitimate-seeming websites and social-media identities. They also leave little apparent trace of their whereabouts. The woman who called herself Ms. Ilic, for example, arrived and left in an ordinary taxi and paid for the meal with cash, Mr. Irons said.

Roddy Boyd, who has written articles for his Southern Investigative Reporting Foundation that raised questions about charitable foundations run by AmTrust's founders, said he received two unusual approaches.

A few months ago, he said, a man claiming to be calling from London offered to donate money to his journalistic foundation, in return for answering questions about AmTrust. Mr. Boyd said he provided a few generalities about the company and told the man not to donate.

In mid-2016, Mr. Boyd said, he traveled to London to meet a man identifying himself as a Hong Kong-based adviser to a wealthy family interested in starting an Asian journalism venture. The consultant, he recalls, asked detailed questions about his articles on AmTrust founders.

Mr. Boyd said he later discovered the consultant's background had holes; his Hong Kong phone number has since been disconnected. "Something was very wrong," Mr. Boyd said. He said the consultant was armed with a printout of an AmTrust critique by Mr. Nelson, the short seller.

Mr. Nelson, of Dallas-based Alistair Capital Management LLC, said he was contacted around the same time by a London-based consultant, purporting to represent a Middle Eastern investor looking to hire Mr. Nelson's firm to manage at least $50 million.

He said the man offered to fly him to London to discuss the offer. Mr. Nelson said he was suspicious and declined the offer. The consultant's email address is no longer active; his website domain was registered to a mailbox drop address.

The Israeli firm employed by AmTrust in the Italian matter, Black Cube, has an office in London. Black Cube declined to comment, saying it can't discuss clients "or the work we have done for them, if any." The firm said its work always complies with local laws.

GeoInvesting co-founder Dan David  Mr. Irons's boss this month filed a civil suit in a Pennsylvania state court against the woman who identified herself as Ms. Ilic and a second purported consultant who contacted Mr. David in May, also asking questions about AmTrust. AmTrust wasn't named in the suit.

Though this second consultant said he was French, Mr. David whose wife is French believed the man spoke English with an Israeli accent. The consultant didn't return calls or emails sent to the contact information he gave Mr. David. The legal action allowed Mr. David to get subpoenas for security-camera footage of the meetings with the consultants, in the hope of identifying them.

Pakistani Bank to Shut N.Y. Branch Amid Money-Laundering Dispute

By Samuel Rubenfeld

A Pakistani bank said Monday it will close its New York branch amid a dispute with the state's financial regulator over whether it's improved its anti-money-laundering compliance program.

Habib Bank, which has had a branch in the state since 1978, has compliance failures that are "serious, persistent and apparently affect the entire Habib banking enterprise," the New York State Department of Financial Services alleged in a statement dated Aug. 24 ordering the bank to appear at a hearing in September. The NYDFS alleged 53 violations stretching back over the course of a decade, and said it intended to impose a $629.6 million penalty.

"The bank's recent misconduct has produced grave risks to itself, to banking institutions in New York State and the U.S. and to the financial system as a whole," the NYDFS said. "Although the bank has been given more than sufficient opportunity to rectify its deficiencies, it has utterly failed to do so, demonstrating a sheer inability to accomplish remediation, a stubborn unwillingness to do so, or both."

Karachi-based Habib Bank, which is also known globally as HBL, said on Monday in a statement announcing the closure of its New York branch that the NYDFS "is still not appreciating or recognizing the significant progress" the branch made despite "sincere and extensive remediation measures" taken in the time since an order to do so in 2015 by the Federal Reserve and NYDFS.

The bank said Monday it would "vigorously contest" the proposed civil penalty at the administrative hearing, and in U.S. courts, "as being unjustified, capricious, unreasonable, not supported by facts or law and as being time barred."

Representatives at the New York branch of Habib Bank weren't available to comment.

Habib Bank's New York branch mainly offers U.S.-dollar clearing services. The bank said the closure wouldn't have a material effect on its business outside the U.S.

The NYDFS alleged in its statement of hearing that Habib Bank struggled to comply with New York banking laws since 2006, and that it has been plagued by "repeated breakdowns" in its sanctions and money laundering compliance programs, with violations occurring every year since that time other than in 2009.

Habib Bank was also subject to an exam in March 2016 cited in the NYDFS statement that had found "significant weaknesses" in the New York branch's risk management capabilities, and that, despite repeated criticism from the NYDFS, "management had yet to implement effective controls" to mitigate and manage its money laundering and sanctions risks.

The NYDFS also said, in separate orders dated Aug. 24, that its probe of Habib Bank's compliance programs would continue after the New York branch's closure, and that it would expand the window of transactions it seeks to review for potential sanctions-compliance violations.

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

Trading Spikes in Gasoline ETF

Trading in the United States Gasoline Fund (UGA) soars to the highest in almost six years after Hurricane Harvey knocks out refining operations in Texas. The exchange-traded fund, which invests in gasoline futures, saw almost 500,000 shares change hands as of this afternoon as gasoline prices surged. Nearly 15% of US refinery capacity had been disrupted by the storm as of this morning, raising the potential for fuel shortages. Gasoline futures for September delivery gained 4.57 cents per gallon, or 2.74%, to $1.7123. The gasoline ETF up 2.5% to $27.50 per share. (asjylyn.loder@wsj.com; stephanie.yang@wsj.com; alison.sider@wsj.com)

J.P. Morgan Takes Stab At Predicting Harvey's Economic Impact

J.P. Morgan's Michael Feroli says in a note that Hurricane Harvey is likely to have the effect such storms often bring: Economic pain on the front end as the damage is taken, with added growth in the recovery stage. His firm estimates economic damage of $10 billion to $20 billion, which would be much less than Hurricane Katrina. "We believe the overall impact on GDP in Q3 and Q4 will be quite small, consistent with the historical experience. For this reason, we are not changing our top-line GDP forecast," Feroli writes. (michael.derby@wsj.com)

Harvey's Wind, Storm Surge Causing $1.2B to $2.3B in Damage

Hurricane Harvey's winds and storm surge will likely cause $1.2 billion to $2.3 billion in insured losses, says catastrophe modeling firm AIR Worldwide, owned by Verisk Analytics (VRSK). Most of that damage hit Rockport, Texas, and surrounding towns. The estimate does not include the cost of damage from torrential rain and flooding that has devastated Houston. Another 10-20 inches of rain are expected by Friday, AIR says. (nicole.friedman@wsj.com; @NicoleFriedman)

Wells Fargo Donates $1M to Support Hurricane Harvey Relief

Wells Fargo (WFC) says it's donating $1 million to support Hurricane Harvey relief, with about $500K going to the American Red Cross Disaster Relief Fund and another $500K to local nonprofits focused on recovery and relief efforts. "With forecasts calling for more rain and potentially more flooding, we will continue to work with nonprofits and those focused on relief efforts," David Miree, lead region bank president, says. WFC says customers can donate to the American Red Cross's hurricane relief effort at its ATMs across the country starting Tuesday. Customers won't be charged a fee to use the service and 100% of the donations will be sent to the American Red Cross. The bank also says certain rewards customers can redeem rewards and donate to the American Red Cross through Sept. 12. WFC is waiving ATM fees for customers in affected areas and will reverse other fees, such as late fees, for all its consumer products, including credit cards and checking accounts. (emily.glazer@wsj.com; @EmilyGlazerWSJ)

Property Insurance Stocks Fall on Harvey

Shares of property insurance companies are falling following Hurricane Harvey. Travelers (TRV) is down 2.8%, the biggest decliner in the Dow industrials. Other big movers are Axis Capital Holdings (AXS), down 2.8%, Aspen Insurance Holdings (AHL), down 2.7%, and XL Group (XL), off 2.7%. All three are Bermuda-based companies that sell specialty insurance and reinsurance, sectors that are expected to see relatively larger payouts than other parts of the industry. Another decliner was car insurer Progressive (PGR), off 2.5%. PGR has about 9% of the auto insurance market in Texas, according to data provider SNL Financial. (erik.holm@wsj.com; @erikholmwsj)

Last-Resort Texas Insurer Could Be Tested

All eyes are on the Texas Windstorm Insurance Association, the insurer of last resort for windstorm and hail insurance on the Texas coast. TWIA offers coverage to residential and commercial properties in certain regions where private insurance is deemed unavailable. The association's payment capacity for this year is $4.9 billion, and it has the option to charge policyholders more if claims exceed its payment capacity, Moody's says. (nicole.friedman@wsj.com; @NicoleFriedman)

Harvey Hits Home For Houston Oil Community

Hurricane Harvey didn't just hit oil infrastructure, it also hit oil people, as Houston and surrounding areas is home to hundreds of thousands of oil professionals, oilfield workers, brokers, analysts and more. Firms like Houston-based energy investment bank and research firm Tudor Pickering Holt find themselves analyzing the financial side of the story to find winners and losers, while also pitching in on relief and recovery. "TPH paired trade: Long helping our Houston area neighbors, Short Hurricane Harvey," it says. "TPH will donate half of this week's trading commissions to Harvey relief and recovery efforts in the Houston area." (dan.molinski@wsj.com)

Tens of Thousands of Harvey Insurance Claims Expected

Homeowners could file up to 50,000 claims with private insurers for wind damage following Hurricane Harvey and more than 100,000 claims for flood coverage from the National Flood Insurance Program, the Consumer Federation of America says. The CFA estimates that insured losses from wind damage could reach $2 billion, and flood claims could reach more than $5 billion. Total flood damage could be similar to Hurricane Katrina, the CFA says, but the insurance payouts will be lower than the 2005 storm because many Houston homes don't have flood insurance. (nicole.friedman@wsj.com; @NicoleFriedman)

Suits Over Flood Exclusion in Homeowners' Policies Likely

Harvey's massive flooding damage is likely to prompt many efforts by plaintiffs' lawyers to find ways to pierce through the flooding exclusion that is part of standard homeowners' policies, to get payouts for hard-hit families, say industry lawyers and analysts. "I have no doubt that right now there are lawyers in Texas reading every Texas court decision addressing flood coverage to find a way around the flood exclusion," said Randy Maniloff, an insurance-industry lawyer in Philadelphia. He said the exclusion is strong, but there is "too much at stake for people not to take a run at it." (leslie.scism@wsj.com)

Glacial Pace of Business Lending Growth Continues

Business loans grew at 2% from a year earlier, according to the latest Federal Reserve data released on Friday. The metric is slightly up from a few weeks earlier, but remains far below the pre-election pace. Between mid-2014 and mid-2016, commercial-and-industrial loan growth often grew at a double-digit pace. The paradox is that bank stocks have gone up so much since the election, even though the key metric of loan growth has continued to slow down. Bank executives have repeatedly said that business customers are waiting for more clarity from Washington before making investments. The question is whether investors will continue to patiently wait for the supposed pent-up demand to materialize. (rachel.ensign@wsj.com)

CEO of Santander Holdings Takes Over Auto-Loan Unit

Scott Powell is succeeding Jason Kulas as president and CEO as Santander Consumer USA Holdings (SC) effective immediately, the company says. This publicly traded consumer finance unit is a part of Santander Holdings USA, which is a unit of Spanish lender Banco Santander, S.A. Powell is already chief executive of Santander Holdings USA, and will keep that title. The consumer-finance unit focuses on auto lending, an area that analysts are concerned will face rising defaults. Also, Santander Consumer last year said it had to restate its financial results. (Rachel.Ensign@wsj.com)

Postal Savings Bank Gets A+ Rating From Fitch

Fitch Ratings awards Postal Savings Bank of China (1658.HK), the country's No. 6 lender by assets, with an A+ long-term issuer default rating and with Stable Outlook, to reflect its "government-protected monopoly" and "larger policy role than other state banks." Despite lower profitability and capitalization than some peers, Postal Savings Bank can benefit from strong funding and liquidity provided by its extensive retail network. With 1H17 results due out tomorrow, the stock is up 1.3% at HK$4.69 in late trading Monday, yet that's still 1.5% below its IPO price. (john.wu@wsj.com)

Hurricane Harvey Expected to Bring Huge New Claims

It will take days, if not weeks or longer, for flood insurance claims to emerge from Hurricane Harvey.

Meanwhile, the National Flood Insurance Program is set to expire at the end of September and has just $5.8 billion left it can borrow from the Treasury to meet new claims, according to January figures reported to Congress.

The program already has a debt of roughly $25 billion from earlier weather disasters. Much of that is from Hurricane Katrina in 2005 and superstorm Sandy in 2012. Sandy alone cost $8.4 billion initially, according to the Federal Emergency Management Agency, which manages the program. The program had to borrow a further $1.6 billion from the Treasury in January just to pay interest on its debt and storm claims, according to FEMA.

Prime Borrowers Were the Prime Defaulters in 2007 Mortgage Bust

Most of the rise in mortgage debt and delinquencies during the 2007 crisis was driven by mid- to high-credit score borrowers, rather than the subprime sector, according to "http://www.nber.org/papers/w23740" published by the National Bureau of Economic Research. The growth in defaults is mostly accounted for by real-estate investors holding two or more first mortgages, the researchers write. Real-estate investors were more likely to use nonconventional mortgages that are intrinsically riskier, and they were also likely to prefer highly leveraged products, the researchers say.

It's Tough Being a Bank Director

The Federal Reserve is right to revamp supervisory expectations for bank directors, but its approach misses two important factors, writes Thomas P. Vartanian in a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/why-would-anyone-sane-be-a-bank-director-1503960852". First, the overhaul doesn't focus enough on the biggest challenge for directors striking a balance between turning a profit and taking on risk, says Mr. Vartanian, a partner at law firm Dechert LLP and a former general counsel of the Federal Home Loan Bank Board. Second, he says, the Fed doesn't address "the all-important endgame for directors: When and how are they held financially accountable if their institutions fail?"

Fiduciary-Rule Delays Boost Wall Street at Consumers' Expense

The Trump administration's efforts to derail the Labor Department's fiduciary rule could be due to "fear on Wall Street that consumers and regulators might someday demand similar, customer-first standards for all financial advisers, not just those handling retirement accounts," "http://nyti.ms/2xs2ixK". "Wall Street wants to keep selling investments that make the most money for Wall Street, and it can't do that if strong fiduciary standards are in place," the board says. Investor advocates might sue the administration if it attempts to water down the rule, but that could lead to further delays, according to the editorial.

Fed Officials Should Steer Clear of Politics

Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen's defense of postcrisis financial regulations last week, coupled with earlier comments in the same vein from Fed No. 2 Stanley Fischer, show that "Fed officials are launching a political campaign to retain their vast discretionary control over the American financial system," writes "https://www.wsj.com/articles/our-political-central-bankers-1503961596". Banks are safer now than before the crisis, the editorial says, but that makes sense after years of economic placidity. A better approach is "a financial regime with high levels of capital to defend against potential losses but with less regulatory micro-managing," the board writes.

The House Ethics Committee said it is investigating Rep. Chris Collins (R., N.Y.) after an advocacy group requested a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/APc33d5b4cd9694926be7d56661ed0f5e8".

The stock market is having a good year. "https://blogs.wsj.com/moneybeat/2017/08/28/bitcoin-valued-like-a-blue-chip-trading-like-a-small-cap/" At the end of last week, the digital currency was up 352% year to date.

Pro Private Markets
VC Profile: Startups Weigh Pros, Cons of Alexa Fund

By Patience Haggin 
800 words
29 August 2017
14:18
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Last year, Amazon.com Inc. led a Series A round in Nucleus, a startup making a home video-conferencing system designed to integrate with Amazon's voice-controlled artificial-intelligence assistant Alexa.

In May, Amazon announced the Echo Show, an Alexa-enabled video chat device with similar functionality too similar, say Nucleus co-founders. In recent interviews with other publications they alleged Amazon took advantage of their partnership to raid Nucleus's ideas and development process.

Alexa Fund

The Firm

Amazon unveiled the Alexa Fund in 2015 with $100 million.

The Team

The fund operates out of Amazon's corporate development team, led by Director of Worldwide Corporate Development Paul Bernard.

The Strategy

Amazon's fund backs startups working on voice technologies, including those developing new capabilities for the Alexa Skills Kit or the Alexa Voice Service.

Recent Deals

The Alexa Fund recently invested in a $300 million round for smartphone maker Essential Products Inc. and a $50 million Series B round for tracking device maker TrackR Inc.

"We've been working on Echo Show and Alexa calling and messaging for a long time," said an Amazon spokeswoman. "Work on both was already well underway before we had our first discussion with Nucleus and was not influenced by those discussions."

Nucleus, whose legal name is CloudTalk Inc., is among the more than 20 startups Amazon has backed from its $100 million Alexa Fund. The fund represents one of several efforts by Amazon to cultivate a developer ecosystem around its voice-controlled artificial intelligence assistant, which Amazon hopes will become the hub of the American "smart home." Amazon's backing comes with risks and rewards, said entrepreneurs and investors racing to fill the "smart home" with voice-controlled, internet-connected products.

Amazon declined to make the fund's investing team available for comment.

Startups backed by Alexa Fund get special access to the Amazon mothership. Such companies receive some early peeks at the road map for Alexa, as well as inside help in designing and implementing Alexa-integrated features, said both the Amazon spokeswoman and chief executive officers of portfolio companies. For example, the companies had early notice that Amazon would introduce programming tools to connect a camera, said Jamie Siminoff, CEO of Bot Home Automation Inc., the maker of a connected video doorbell that does business as Ring. "You get access to a person who can help you navigate Amazon," said Chris Herbert, CEO of tracking device maker TrackR Inc. He said his company worked with Amazon staff to develop TrackR's user experience and beta test its Alexa skills.

Even with those rewards on the table, some venture capitalists said they would advise companies to be wary about accepting Amazon's investment, because of the risk of Amazon copying ideas.

It isn't new for corporate venture-capital programs to get ideas from the startups they back, and even imitate them. With Amazon, a company that has designs on just about every industry, the concerns are bigger, said investors who watch the market for home Internet-of-Things products. A central concern is that information shared with the Alexa Fund team may make its way to Amazon's first-party product team, these investors said.

But the Alexa Fund has "measures in place" to ensure portfolio companies' confidential information is treated appropriately, the Amazon spokeswoman said. Still, the fund does introduce portfolio companies to other Amazon departments including the Alexa product team often at the companies' request, she said.

"We do not incorporate any Alexa Fund portfolio companies' confidential information into Amazon products we independently design and build our own devices and work with select Alexa Fund portfolio companies around licensed integrations of their technology," said the Amazon spokeswoman.

Venrock Partner David Pakman said, "As a venture investor and former startup CEO, you have to be paranoid." Mr. Pakman said he has invested in several startups that have had conversations with the Alexa Fund. Many investors who back smart home devices urged caution, but said they wouldn't tell companies to rule out accepting Amazon's backing.

"You always have to be careful with Amazon. Anybody that big and that aggressive, you don't want to underestimate," said David Wu, a general partner at venture firm Maveron. Still, he added, "For the startups I've seen that have taken Alexa investment, it has opened doors."

CEOs of Alexa Fund portfolio companies said they believe the relationship benefits their companies.

"We believe that it's better to engage big scary monsters, rather than to run away," TrackR's Mr. Herbert said. "It's about thinking about what information we want to hold to the chest, and what we can disclose when you create a partnership."

Pro Private Markets
Amplero's AI-Driven Move to Amp Up Marketers; Software can automatically refine marketing campaigns

By Patience Haggin 
337 words
29 August 2017
12:30
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Amplero Inc., a startup developing software that uses artificial intelligence for marketing efforts, has raised $17.5 million in Series B funding.

Greycroft Partners and Ignition Partners co-led the round as new investors in the company. Returning investors Wildcat Venture Partners, Seven Peaks Ventures and Trilogy Equity Partners also participated.

Seattle-based Amplero makes software that uses artificial intelligence to help marketing teams make decisions about where and how to focus their outreach efforts for current customers. Amplero's software integrates with tools such as Hootsuite and Twilio in efforts to automate marketers' interactions with those tools.

"Our algorithm takes the information based on what it's learned and automatically refines the marketing campaign, targeting the right audience," said Chief Executive Olly Downs.

Salesforce Ventures has backed Amplero in the past. Mr. Down said the marketing software giant has facilitated a number of introductions on Amplero's behalf.

"The dream of a marketer is to get to one-to-one or totally personalized marketing," said Bob Kelly, managing partner at Ignition Partners.

With his investment in Amplero, Mr. Kelly and other venture capitalists who participated in the round are betting on a once-crowded sector where "https://www.wsj.com/articles/funding-for-advertising-and-marketing-plummets-in-2016-as-m-a-heats-up-1482928203".

Venture-capital investment in marketing and advertising startups saw a boom from 2011 to 2014, while venture capitalists bet on companies that could be acquisition targets for Google and Facebook as they filled their technology stacks. Investment in the sector has fallen dramatically since.

Venture capitalists backed 75 U.S. marketing and advertising startups in the first half of 2017, down more than 40% from the same period of 2014.

Amplero has about 45 employees. The company plans to hire and have more than 60 employees by year's end, Mr. Downs said. The company has raised about $25 million in total, including the new round.
