Sale of Once Hot High-Frequency Trading Firm Reflects Industry Troubles

By Alexander Osipovich

They shook up Wall Street, changing the way the financial industry trades stocks, bonds and derivatives. Now, high-speed traders are in retreat.

These upstart firms use sophisticated computer algorithms to move in and out of stocks, futures and other positions in fractions of a second. Known as high-frequency traders, or HFT, they thrived in the years following the financial crisis by exploiting the markets' big price swings.

After big Wall Street banks lost business to these more nimble traders, they copied them and began to introduce HFT-style algos and technology on their own expansive trading floors.

But more recently, there have been fewer dramatic swings in stocks, commodities and other markets. The CBOE Volatility Index, a widely followed measure of expected U.S. stock-market volatility, has hovered near historic lows this year.

Now, one electronic trading firm's deal to acquire a struggling rival shows how this persistently low volatility is upending the HFT world and forcing out weaker players.

DRW Holdings LLC, a Chicago-based firm with one of the largest high-speed trading platforms, said on Wednesday it is acquiring RGM Advisors LLC. The two privately held firms declined to disclose the terms of the deal, which they expect to close next month.

RGM turned big profits in the years after the financial crisis, say people close to the firm. It was once among the most visible firms in the often secretive high-speed trading world, where companies jealously guard their algorithms.

During its peak years after the crisis, RGM accounted for as much as 5% of U.S. equities trading volume, said a person close to the firm. Walls in the firm's downtown Austin, Tx., office were decorated with cartoonish images of robots.

But while the lack of volatility has provided a favorable backdrop for stock markets that climb to new highs, it has been disastrous for the once-thriving HFT business.

A costly high-tech arms race in which HFT firms relentlessly compete to shave tiny fractions of a second off data transmission times has also hurt their bottom lines.

Revenue at HFT firms from U.S. equities trading is projected to be just $850 million this year, compared to $7.2 billion in revenue from U.S. stocks trading in 2009, Tabb Group says.

Techniques pioneered by the upstart traders are now common at old-line financial firms. Goldman Sachs, for example, had 600 human traders supporting its cash equities business in 2000. That number is down to a handful, the bank said recently. They are supported by hundreds of engineers.

Big Wall Street banks now plug directly into exchanges' data centers, speeding up each trade by milliseconds, to better execute trades for institutional clients. HFT firms were initially the heaviest users of such "co-location" services.

RGM was among the innovators, but its business deteriorated amid the recent lack of market volatility. The firm made around $800 million in trading profits over its lifetime, but this year it has been losing money, prompting its owners to accelerate efforts to find a buyer, a person close to the firm said. RGM has also cut staff to around 70 employees, down from more than 100 a few years ago.

This week, RGM agreed to sell the firm for what one person close to the deal said was less than $10 million.

Both DRW and RGM declined to comment on the deal's size.

But DRW founder and chief executive Donald Wilson Jr. said his firm will be among the survivors.

"DRW is positioned to continue to perform well in a variety of market conditions because we are diversified across risk holding periods and asset classes," he wrote in an emailed statement.

RGM becomes the latest firm to succumb to a wave of consolidation that has whittled down HFT firms' ranks. In July, Virtu Financial Inc. completed a $1.4 billion acquisition of rival KCG Holdings. The combined firm handles around one in five shares traded in U.S. equity markets each day.

Others have scaled back or shut down. Thomas Peterffy, chairman and chief executive of Interactive Brokers Group Inc., said in March he would end his firm's options market-making activities. Two Sigma Securities agreed to buy the business in May.

"We're still in the midst of the industry consolidating and reorganizing itself," said Eric Pritchett, chief executive of Potamus Trading LLC, an electronic trading firm based in Boston. "I don't think we're done."

RGM was founded in 2001 by three men: Robbie Robinette, who had studied physics at the University of Texas at Austin; Richard Gorelick, a lawyer by training; and Mark Melton, a software developer with a specialty in artificial intelligence. They chose the name "RGM" after their initials, launching operations in Mr. Robinette's living room.

The firm made $17 on its first day of trading in December 2001, which its founders used to buy three margaritas, according to an interview with Mr. Robinette posted on RGM's website. RGM eventually expanded into futures, currencies, fixed income and European and Japanese equities.

Mr. Gorelick, who is also the chief executive, defended high-speed trading when it came under attack in Michael Lewis's 2014 book "Flash Boys." The book, which was published as RGM and many of its peers were starting their decline, attacked HFT firms for allegedly taking advantage of slower-moving investors. Mr. Gorelick also testified on Capitol Hill last year to lobby against proposed regulations for algorithmic trading firms.

In 2013, The Wall Street Journal reported that RGM had held talks with Chicago-based Allston Trading LLC about a possible merger. The deal never came to fruition, people familiar with the situation said. Allston declined to comment.

The next year, RGM closed its London office. It moved to liquidate what remained of its European trading subsidiary last November, U.K. company filings show.

Appeared in the August 17, 2017, print edition as  Speed Traders Whittle Down.'

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

U.K. Audit Regulator Fines PwC, Senior Auditor

The Financial Reporting Council, the U.K. regulator for audit, accounting and reporting, has fined PricewaterhouseCoopers and a senior auditor in relation to the audit of financial statements of RSM Tenon. PwC and Nicholas Boden, senior statutory auditor, admitted their audit of the company's 2011 statements fell short of standards, according to the FRC. PwC agreed to pay GBP5.6M ($7.2M), in part to cover the FRC's costs. Boden was fined GBP114,750. Both PwC and Boden were reprimanded, the FRC says. (nina.trentmann@wsj.com; @Nina_Trentmann)

Cyber Risk Still Concern After Bangladesh Heist

Federal Reserve Bank of New York markets chief Simon Potter says in remarks to an association of African central banks that cyberattacks in banking were a continued concern. He says an attack on Bangladesh Bank last year, which led to the Fed mistakenly sending out payments on fraudulent but authenticated messages hackers sent over the Society for Worldwide Interbank Financial Telecommunication, or Swift, network showed the incident is "a matter that concerns the entire central bank community." Swift has rolled out a series of measures to improve controls, he adds, and an industry task force has been set up to ensure better security in interbank payments, including in Africa. (katy.burne@wsj.com)

U.S. Housing Starts Decline Despite Strong Confidence

U.S. housing starts declined for the fourth time in five months in July, the Commerce Department says, a sign that a driver of recent economic strength is cooling. Starts reached a post-recession peak in October 2016. Since then the pace of building has slowed despite strong confidence among builders and consumers, rising stock values and low unemployment all factors that should support construction. The July data is a worrisome sign that building slowdown wasn't a spring-time blip, but rather indicates weaker demand. A similar phenomenon appears to be playing out in the automotive industry. And while apartment and condominium building slowed most sharply, starts for single-family homes have also declined four of the past five months. (eric.morath@wsj.com; @ericmorath)

Danske Invest Strengthens Sales in Germany

Asset management firm Danske Invest switches to a higher gear in Germany, its key market besides Scandinavian countries. It has appointed Markus Banna into the newly created position of director for business development, effective Wednesday, with a focus on institutional clients. Mr. Banna previously held positions at Prime Capital, Frankfurt-Trust and SEB, and was also a fund manager at Invesco Asset Management and Dresdner Bank. Danske Invest had assets under management of around EUR90 billion as of December 31, 2016. (emese.bartha@wsj.com; @EmeseBartha)

Rocky Path to Greek Bad-Loan Reduction

The reduction of Greek banks' non-performing loans is an expensive endeavor, with UBS analysts expecting higher write-offs and elevated-loan loss provisions until 2019 as lenders race to meet Bank of Greece and Single Supervisory Mechanism targets. The high level of non-performing loans still sitting on domestic banks' balance sheets is a factor that weighs on their credit quality, even as the economy starts showing signs of a recovery, Moody's said when upgrading large Greek banks' ratings in June. (tasos.vossos@wsj.com, @tasosvos)

China Banks' Net Forex Sales Dropped in July

Chinese banks sold a net $15.5 billion of foreign exchange last month, versus $20.9 billion in June, according to the country's forex regulator. That as cross-boarder capital flows were largely steady in July, reflecting stabilizing expectations of the yuan, the State Administration of Foreign Exchange added. (liyan.qi@wsj.com)

States Step In for Workers Without Retirement Plans

States are forging ahead with efforts to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/states-test-workplace-retirement-plans-for-residents-lacking-them-1502881230", as debate intensifies over government's role in ensuring people can support themselves in their later years.

Oregon this summer became the first state to start requiring employers that don't offer a retirement plan of their own to give employees access to a state-run plan, by automatically enrolling them in individual retirement accounts invested in mutual funds. Eight other states are designing similar programs, two of which California and Illinois are expected to launch initiatives next year.

Proponents of state-run retirement programs say they are concerned about the estimated 42% of private-sector workers who don't have access to a workplace retirement-savings plan, many of whom don't save at all. State legislators also are trying to save taxpayers money over the long term by reducing retirees' reliance on public assistance programs, including Medicaid.

Student Borrowers Owe More, Struggle to Repay Even Small Loans

More than 40% of student-loan borrowers leave school owing at least $20,000, up from a 20% level 15 years ago, according to "http://bit.ly/2vE5EPm". "A considerable portion of borrowers continue to carry nearly the entire amount they borrowed several years into repayment," according to the report. In addition, many borrowers with relatively small loans have recently become less likely to "actively pay down their loans," the report says, which is "striking given the growth in the usage of alternative repayment plans in the last several years."

The U.K.'s Financial Conduct Authority has proposed making it easier for state-owned companies to list on the London Stock Exchange, in a bid to attract a listing by oil giant Saudi Aramco. The move puts investors at risk, with implications beyond the Saudi listing, "http://bit.ly/2i6DXKr". State-owned enterprises carry particular governance risks exemplified by the investigation into Brazil's Petrobras and the retreat of Russian and Central Asian companies from the LSE in recent years and it's unclear whether investors are able to assess the additional risk, says Ms. Amico, managing director of the Economic and Corporate Governance Center.

Now those deals are fraying, and local regulators are monitoring its marketing of insurance products.

Markets
Sale of Once Hot High-Frequency Trading Firm Reflects Industry Troubles; DRW buying high-frequency trader RGM is the latest sign of how the long period of low volatility is forcing out weaker players

By Alexander Osipovich 
1,031 words
16 August 2017
22:06
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

They shook up Wall Street, changing the way the financial industry trades stocks, bonds and derivatives. Now, high-speed traders are in retreat.

These upstart firms use sophisticated computer algorithms to move in and out of stocks, futures and other positions in fractions of a second. Known as high-frequency traders, or HFT, they thrived in the years following the financial crisis by exploiting the markets' big price swings.

After big Wall Street banks lost business to these more nimble traders, they copied them and began to introduce HFT-style algos and technology on their own expansive trading floors.

But more recently, there have been fewer dramatic swings in stocks, commodities and other markets. The CBOE Volatility Index, a widely followed measure of expected U.S. stock-market volatility, has hovered near historic lows this year.

Now, one electronic trading firm's deal to acquire a struggling rival shows how this "http://www.wsj.com/articles/high-frequency-traders-fall-on-hard-times-1490092200"and forcing out weaker players.

DRW Holdings LLC, a Chicago-based firm with one of the largest high-speed trading platforms, said on Wednesday it is acquiring RGM Advisors LLC. The two privately held firms declined to disclose the terms of the deal, which they expect to close next month.

RGM turned big profits in the years after the financial crisis, say people close to the firm. It was once among the most visible firms in the often secretive high-speed trading world, where companies jealously guard their algorithms.

During its peak years after the crisis, RGM accounted for as much as 5% of U.S. equities trading volume, said a person close to the firm. Walls in the firm's downtown Austin, Tx., office were decorated with cartoonish images of robots.

But while the lack of volatility has provided a favorable backdrop for stock markets that climb to new highs, it has been disastrous for the once-thriving HFT business.

A costly high-tech arms race in which HFT firms relentlessly compete to shave tiny fractions of a second off data transmission times has also hurt their bottom lines.

Revenue at HFT firms from U.S. equities trading is projected to be just $850 million this year, compared to $7.2 billion in revenue from U.S. stocks trading in 2009, Tabb Group says.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/how-does-high-frequency-trading-affect-individual-investors-1408454495"are now common at old-line financial firms. Goldman Sachs, for example, had 600 human traders supporting its cash equities business in 2000. That number is down to a handful, the bank said recently. They are supported by hundreds of engineers.

Big Wall Street banks now plug directly into exchanges' data centers, speeding up each trade by milliseconds, to better execute trades for institutional clients. HFT firms were initially the heaviest users of such "co-location" services.

RGM was among the innovators, but its business deteriorated amid the recent lack of market volatility. The firm made around $800 million in trading profits over its lifetime, but this year it has been losing money, prompting its owners to accelerate efforts to find a buyer, a person close to the firm said. RGM has also cut staff to around 70 employees, down from more than 100 a few years ago.

This week, RGM agreed to sell the firm for what one person close to the deal said was less than $10 million.

Both DRW and RGM declined to comment on the deal's size.

But DRW founder and chief executive Donald Wilson Jr. said his firm will be among the survivors.

"DRW is positioned to continue to perform well in a variety of market conditions because we are diversified across risk holding periods and asset classes," he wrote in an emailed statement.

RGM becomes the latest firm to succumb to a wave of consolidation that has whittled down HFT firms' ranks. In July, Virtu Financial Inc. completed"https://www.wsj.com/articles/virtu-financial-agrees-to-buy-kcg-holdings-for-1-4-billion-1492689363"KCG Holdings. The combined firm handles around one in five shares traded in U.S. equity markets each day.

Others have scaled back or shut down. Thomas Peterffy, chairman and chief executive of Interactive Brokers Group Inc., said in March he would end his firm's options market-making activities. Two Sigma Securities"https://www.wsj.com/articles/two-sigma-agrees-to-buy-interactive-brokers-options-market-making-arm-1494367672" in May.

"We're still in the midst of the industry consolidating and reorganizing itself," said Eric Pritchett, chief executive of Potamus Trading LLC, an electronic trading firm based in Boston. "I don't think we're done."

RGM was founded in 2001 by three men: Robbie Robinette, who had studied physics at the University of Texas at Austin; Richard Gorelick, a lawyer by training; and Mark Melton, a software developer with a specialty in artificial intelligence. They chose the name "RGM" after their initials, launching operations in Mr. Robinette's living room.

The firm made $17 on its first day of trading in December 2001, which its founders used to buy three margaritas, according to an interview with Mr. Robinette posted on RGM's website. RGM eventually expanded into futures, currencies, fixed income and European and Japanese equities.

Mr. Gorelick, who is also the chief executive, defended high-speed trading when it came under attack in "https://www.wsj.com/articles/book-review-flash-boys-by-michael-lewis-1396308551" The book, which was published as RGM and many of its peers were starting their decline, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/holder-to-tell-lawmakers-u-s-is-investigating-high-speed-trading-1396618144?tesla=y"for allegedly taking advantage of slower-moving investors. Mr. Gorelick also testified on Capitol Hill last year to lobby against proposed regulations for algorithmic trading firms.

In 2013, The Wall Street Journal reported that "https://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424127887323836504578553662434691152"with Chicago-based Allston Trading LLC about a possible merger. The deal never came to fruition, people familiar with the situation said. Allston declined to comment.

The next year, RGM closed its London office. It moved to liquidate what remained of its European trading subsidiary last November, U.K. company filings show.

Pro Private Markets
Artemis Health Gets $16 Million Injection for Health Benefits Data Platform; Round marks company's second raise in 12 months

By Heather Mack 
387 words
16 August 2017
12:00
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Artemis Health, Inc., a provider of a health data analytics platform for employee benefits managers, has raised $16 million in Series B funding led by F-Prime Capital.

Returning investors Maverick Ventures and Kickstart Seed Fund also contributed.

Salt Lake City-based Artemis's software allows employers offering employees insurance the ability to visualize where their health care spending is going. Through a series of apps, employers can identify inefficiencies, overspending or gaps in care, then receive suggested fixes such as implementing a telemedicine service or changing their prescription coverage plan.

Artemis last received financing at the end of 2016 and had a pre-money valuation of $18.1 million as of May 2017. The latest round brings the company's total funding to date to $26 million.

Artemis's service is similar to that offered by much larger and older companies, such as Towers Watson-acquired Liazon or Truven Health Analytics, which was acquired by IBM Watson Health last year for $2.6 billion. But the company's approach is a bit different in that it focuses on the benefits manager as the target user rather than multiple users such as payers, providers or employees themselves.

"We focus on the benefits people, who are chronically undersupported," said Artemis CEO and founder Grant Gordon. "We do the heavy lifting of data integration and organization so we can offer an approachable user interface with responsive customer service they would expect from consumer-facing software."

It is keeping that user in mind that has led to the company's quick growth, Mr. Gordon said. Since launching, the company has gained 26 customers including large employers like GE, Intuit and AAA, and it has analyzed some $5.8 billion in health care spending.

F-Prime Capital partner Jon Lim, who led the investment and will take a board position, said his decision to back Artemis was influenced by the company's diligence with their customers and response to feedback.

"They've gotten to the point of really understanding on intimate levels the pain points the market is experiencing," said Mr. Lim.

The company plans to use the funding to hire and develop new products.

Fannie, Freddie Sell Off Risk From Mortgages

By Sam Goldfarb 
973 words
15 August 2017
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
A1
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Investors are snapping up a new type of security sold by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, increasingly assuming the risks of mortgage defaults from taxpayers and powering a quiet transformation of the housing giants after almost a decade of government control.

Fannie and Freddie have sold roughly $48 billion of the securities since 2013 to a broadening group of buyers, including asset managers and insurance companies. Sales are expected to reach a fresh high of $15 billion this year, up from the previous record $13 billion last year, according to J.P. Morgan Securities.

The sales mark an early step toward reducing the government's role in the $14.4 trillion U.S. mortgage market. The amount of mortgage debt funneled through Fannie and Freddie and other taxpayer-backed entities roughly doubled after the financial crisis, to around 70%.

The progress has come despite a long-running stalemate in Congress, which has stumbled in its effort to design a replacement for the decades-old housing-finance system that centers on Fannie and Freddie.

It may not be happening as people anticipated, but "the government's footprint in the mortgage market is receding quickly and significantly," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics.

The market for the so-called credit-risk transfers has boomed even as the one for privately issued mortgage-backed securities has remained mostly dormant, a sign that investors have greater comfort in the standards and transparency of these deals than in those issued by Wall Street banks that performed so poorly after the housing bust.

Fannie and Freddie don't make loans, but buy them from lenders and bundle them into securities. Those bonds typically carry a guarantee that Fannie and Freddie will pay investors if the underlying mortgages default, leaving investors with only the risk that the bonds will lose value if interest rates rise.

The credit-risk transfers don't carry that guarantee. Nevertheless, they have proved popular with investors, who have concluded that the yields they offer are worth the added risk. As the market has developed, banks have been willing to trade them, easing concerns that they would need to offer premium yields because they would be difficult for buyers to unload.

Reflecting the strong interest, the average yield that investors have demanded to hold one version of the instrument has fallen by more than half in just two years, to roughly 1.5 percentage points on top of a benchmark floating interest rate, according to J.P. Morgan Securities.

The pool of investors buying the securities is also widening to what many consider more stable sources of capital, a sign of their changing status. While hedge funds made up the largest group of early investors, that distinction now belongs to more traditional money managers, according to J.P. Morgan Securities. Though still small players in the market, insurance companies and real-estate investment trusts have also increased their buying in some recent deals.

Gene Tannuzzo, senior portfolio manager at Columbia Threadneedle, said he bought both Fannie and Freddie risk transfers for the firm's Strategic Income Fund and then sold them as the market rallied.

"Liquidity has definitely improved as more securities have been issued," he said.

The mortgage giants are transferring risk on the loans most likely to default -- those of more than 20 years where homeowners have made less than a 40% down payment. Last year, such mortgages accounted for 60% of all of the single-family mortgages they acquired, according to the Federal Housing Finance Agency, which oversees the companies.

How much risk Fannie and Freddie have shed depends in part on the severity of defaults. In a normal economic expansion, investors would likely shoulder around 20% of the losses in the mortgages underlying the securities, according to a new report by Moody's Analytics. But that number would jump to around 60% to 70% if there were another severe recession comparable to the last one -- enough, if the firms were private, to possibly avoid a federal bailout, according to the Moody's report, which was written by analysts including Mr. Zandi.

The variability is a result of the securities' structure. Each comprises several tranches, linked to a pool of mortgages, with losses from defaults hitting the bottom tranche first and the top tranche last.

On almost all securities, Fannie and Freddie still own the riskiest and the safest tranches, seeing it as uneconomical to sell them to investors. That means they are exposed to mild losses, largely shielded from moderate to severe losses, but vulnerable again once defaults reach a catastrophic level.

The securities are one part of a government effort to revamp the housing giants. Since being placed under conservatorship in 2008, the firms have adopted a more stable business model by shrinking their once huge investment portfolios and raising the fees they charge lenders to guarantee mortgages. They have also joined forces to build a common system for securitizing home loans.

The securities aren't without controversy. Some observers have expressed concerns that Fannie and Freddie are paying investors too much for the risk they are shedding. The securities have also been drawn into a larger debate about what the government should do with Fannie and Freddie, which stand in limbo between the public and private sectors.

---

The amount of new one-to-four family mortgage debt funneled through Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac and other taxpayer-backed entities roughly doubled after the financial crisis, to around 70%. A Page One article Aug. 15 about sales of securities by Fannie and Freddie didn't make clear that the sentence referred to new mortgage debt for one-to-four-family housing units rather than all mortgage debt.

(WSJE September 11, 2017)

Fannie, Freddie Sell Off Risk

By Sam Goldfarb

The credit-risk transfers don't carry that guarantee. Nevertheless, they have proved popular with investors, who have concluded that the yields they offer are worth the added risk. As the market has developed, banks have been willing to trade them, easing concerns that they would need to offer premium yields because they would be difficult for buyers to unload.

Reflecting the strong interest, the average yield that investors have demanded to hold one version of the instrument has fallen by more than half in just two years, to roughly 1.5 percentage points on top of a benchmark floating interest rate, according to J.P. Morgan Securities.

The pool of investors buying the securities is also widening to what many consider more stable sources of capital, a sign of their changing status. While hedge funds made up the largest group of early investors, that distinction now belongs to more traditional money managers, according to J.P. Morgan Securities. Gene Tannuzzo, senior portfolio manager at Columbia Threadneedle, said he bought both Fannie and Freddie risk transfers for the firm's Strategic Income Fund and then sold them as the market rallied.

"Liquidity has definitely improved as more securities have been issued," he said.

Last year, such mortgages accounted for 60% of all of the single-family mortgages they acquired, according to the Federal Housing Finance Agency, which oversees the companies.

How much risk Fannie and Freddie have shed depends in part on the severity of defaults. In a normal economic expansion, investors would likely shoulder around 20% of the losses in the mortgages underlying the securities, according to a new report by Moody's Analytics.

But that number would jump to around 60% to 70% if there were another severe recession comparable to the last one -- enough, if the firms were private, to possibly avoid a federal bailout, according to the Moody's report, which was written by analysts including Mr. Zandi.

The variability is a result of the securities' structure. Each comprises several tranches, linked to a pool of mortgages, with losses from defaults hitting the bottom tranche first and the top tranche last.

That means they are exposed to mild losses, largely shielded from moderate to severe losses, but vulnerable again once defaults reach a catastrophic level.

The securities are one part of a larger government effort to revamp the housing giants. Since being placed under conservatorship in 2008 -- when then-Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson said the government had a responsibility to address their systemic risk -- the firms have adopted a more stable business model by shrinking their once huge investment portfolios and raising the fees they charge lenders to guarantee mortgages. They have also joined forces to build a common system for securitizing home loans.

The securities aren't without controversy. Some observers have expressed concerns that Fannie and Freddie are paying investors too much for the risk they are shedding. Investors could also demand sharply higher yields in a market downturn, forcing Fannie and Freddie to either stop selling the securities, increasing their exposure to future defaults, or raise fees they charge lenders, potentially making home loans more expensive.

The securities have also been drawn into a larger debate about what the government should do with Fannie and Freddie, which stand in limbo between the public and private sectors -- sending nearly all of their profits to the U.S. Treasury despite reporting quarterly earnings and having shares outstanding.

Fannie, Freddie Shift Risk From Taxpayers to Market

By Sam Goldfarb 
1,064 words
15 August 2017

J
A1
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications

The amount of new one-to-four family mortgage debt funneled through Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac and other taxpayer-backed entities roughly doubled after the financial crisis, to around 70%. Fannie and Freddie have sold roughly $48 billion of the securities since 2013, transferring a large measure of risk on roughly one-third of the single-family mortgages they guarantee. The progress has come despite a long-running stalemate in Congress, which has stumbled in its effort to design a replacement for the decades-old housing-finance system that centers on Fannie and Freddie.

It may not be happening as people anticipated, but "the government's footprint in the mortgage market is receding quickly and significantly," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics.

Fannie and Freddie don't make loans, but buy them from lenders and bundle them into securities. The credit-risk transfers don't carry that guarantee. Nevertheless, they have proved popular with investors, who have concluded that the yields they offer are worth the added risk. As the market has developed, banks have been willing to trade them, easing concerns that they would need to offer premium yields because they would be difficult for buyers to unload.

Reflecting the strong interest, the average yield that investors have demanded to hold one version of the instrument has fallen by more than half in just two years, to roughly 1.7 percentage points on top of a benchmark floating interest rate, according to J.P. Morgan Securities.

The pool of investors buying the securities is also widening to what many consider more stable sources of capital, a sign of their changing status. Though still small players in the market, insurance companies and real-estate investment trusts have also increased their buying in some recent deals.

"Liquidity has definitely improved as more securities have been issued," he said.

The mortgage giants are transferring risk on the loans most likely to default -- those of more than 20 years where homeowners have made less than a 40% down payment. Last year, such mortgages accounted for 60% of all of the single-family mortgages they acquired, according to the Federal Housing Finance Agency, which oversees the companies.

How much risk Fannie and Freddie have shed depends in part on the severity of defaults. In a normal economic expansion, investors would likely shoulder around 20% of the losses in the mortgages underlying the securities, according to a new report by Moody's Analytics.

But that number would jump to around 60% to 70% if there were another severe recession comparable to the last one -- enough, if the firms were private, to possibly avoid a federal bailout, according to the Moody's report, which was written by analysts including Mr. Zandi.

The variability is a result of the securities' structure. Each comprises several tranches, linked to a pool of mortgages, with losses from defaults hitting the bottom tranche first and the top tranche last.

On almost all securities, Fannie and Freddie still own the riskiest and the safest tranches, seeing it as uneconomical to sell them to investors. That means they are exposed to mild losses, largely shielded from moderate to severe losses, but vulnerable again if defaults reach a catastrophic level.

The securities are one part of a larger government effort to revamp the housing giants. Since being placed under conservatorship in 2008 -- when then-Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson said the government had a responsibility to address their systemic risk -- the firms have adopted a more stable business model by shrinking their once huge investment portfolios and raising the fees they charge lenders to guarantee mortgages. They have also joined forces to build a common system for securitizing home loans that could ultimately be used by other firms.

The securities aren't without controversy. Some observers have expressed concerns that Fannie and Freddie are paying investors too much for the risk they are shedding. Investors could also demand sharply higher yields in a market downturn, forcing Fannie and Freddie to either stop selling the securities, increasing their exposure to future defaults, or raise fees they charge lenders, potentially making home loans more expensive.

The securities have also been drawn into a larger debate about what the government should do with Fannie and Freddie, which stand in limbo between the public and private sectors -- sending nearly all of their profits to the U.S. Treasury despite reporting quarterly earnings and having shares outstanding.

Some of the securities' strongest advocates include those who want the companies to be dismantled, replaced with a larger number of private firms or a single, government-run utility, which they say would disperse risk and avoid another taxpayer-funded bailout.

Many shareholders, however, want to preserve the companies in near their current form while releasing them from conservatorship. One group has backed a proposal that would provide incentives for the institutions to issue risk transfers without mandating them.

Markets
Investors Take On Mortgage Risk From Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac; Mortgage giants sell $48 billion of securities designed to reduce their exposure

By Sam Goldfarb 
1,116 words
14 August 2017
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications

The amount of new one-to-four family mortgage debt funneled through Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac and other taxpayer-backed entities roughly doubled after the financial crisis, to around 70%. An earlier version of this article didn't make clear that the sentence referred to new mortgage debt for one-to-four-family housing units rather than all mortgage debt. (Sept. 7, 2017)

Investors are snapping up securities sold by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac that shift mortgage default risk away from taxpayers, powering a quiet transformation of the housing giants after almost a decade of government control.

Fannie and Freddie have sold roughly $48 billion of the securities since 2013, transferring a large measure of risk on roughly one-third of the single-family mortgages they guarantee. Sales are expected to reach a fresh high of $15 billion this year, up from the previous record $13 billion last year, according to J.P. Morgan Securities.

The sales mark an early step toward reducing the government's role in the $14.4 trillion U.S. mortgage market. The amount of new one-to-four family mortgage debt funneled through Fannie and Freddie and other taxpayer-backed entities roughly doubled after the financial crisis, to around 70%.

The progress has come despite a long-running stalemate in Congress, which has stumbled in its effort to design a replacement for the decades-old housing-finance system that centers on Fannie and Freddie.

It may not be happening as people anticipated, but "the government's footprint in the mortgage market is receding quickly and significantly," said Mark Zandi, chief economist at Moody's Analytics.

The market for the so-called credit-risk transfers has boomed even as the one for privately issued mortgage-backed securities has remained mostly dormant, a sign that investors have greater comfort in the standards and transparency of these deals than in those issued by Wall Street banks that performed so poorly after the housing bust.

Fannie and Freddie don't make loans, but buy them from lenders and bundle them into securities. Those bonds typically carry a guarantee that Fannie and Freddie will pay investors if the underlying mortgages default, leaving investors with only the risk that the bonds will lose value if interest rates rise.

The credit-risk transfers don't carry that guarantee. Nevertheless, they have proved popular with investors, who have concluded that the yields they offer are worth the added risk. As the market has developed, banks have been willing to trade them, easing concerns that they would need to offer premium yields because they would be difficult for buyers to unload.

Reflecting the strong interest, the average yield that investors have demanded to hold one version of the instrument has fallen by more than half in just two years, to roughly 1.5 percentage points on top of a benchmark floating interest rate, according to J.P. Morgan Securities.

The pool of investors buying the securities is also widening to what many consider more stable sources of capital, a sign of their changing status. While hedge funds made up the largest group of early investors, that distinction now belongs to more traditional money managers, according to J.P. Morgan Securities. Though still small players in the market, insurance companies and real-estate investment trusts have also increased their buying in some recent deals.

Gene Tannuzzo, senior portfolio manager at Columbia Threadneedle, said he bought both Fannie and Freddie risk transfers for the firm's Strategic Income Fund and then sold them as the market rallied.

"Liquidity has definitely improved as more securities have been issued," he said.

The mortgage giants are transferring risk on the loans most likely to default those of more than 20 years where homeowners have made less than a 40% down payment. Last year, such mortgages accounted for 60% of all of the single-family mortgages they acquired, according to the Federal Housing Finance Agency, which oversees the companies.

How much risk Fannie and Freddie have shed depends in part on the severity of defaults. In a normal economic expansion, investors would likely shoulder around 20% of the losses in the mortgages underlying the securities, according to a new report by Moody's Analytics.

But that number would jump to around 60% to 70% if there were another severe recession comparable to the last one enough, if the firms were private, to possibly avoid a federal bailout, according to the Moody's report, which was written by analysts including Mr. Zandi.

The variability is a result of the securities' structure. Each comprises several tranches, linked to a pool of mortgages, with losses from defaults hitting the bottom tranche first and the top tranche last.

On almost all securities, Fannie and Freddie still own the riskiest and the safest tranches, seeing it as uneconomical to sell them to investors. That means they are exposed to mild losses, largely shielded from moderate to severe losses, but vulnerable again once defaults reach a catastrophic level.

The securities are one part of a larger government effort to revamp the housing giants. Since being placed under conservatorship in 2008 when then-Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson said the government had a responsibility to address their systemic risk the firms have adopted a more stable business model by shrinking their once huge investment portfolios and raising the fees they charge lenders to guarantee mortgages. They have also joined forces to build a common system for securitizing home loans that could ultimately be used by other firms.

The securities aren't without controversy. Some observers have expressed concerns that Fannie and Freddie are paying investors too much for the risk they are shedding. Investors could also demand sharply higher yields in a market downturn, forcing Fannie and Freddie to either stop selling the securities, increasing their exposure to future defaults, or raise fees they charge lenders, potentially making home loans more expensive.

The securities have also been drawn into a larger debate about what the government should do with Fannie and Freddie, which stand in limbo between the public and private sectors sending nearly all of their profits to the U.S. Treasury despite reporting quarterly earnings and having shares outstanding.

Some of the securities' strongest advocates include those who want the companies to be dismantled, replaced with a larger number of private firms or a single, government-run utility, which they say would disperse risk and avoid another taxpayer-funded bailout.

Many shareholders, however, want to preserve the companies in near their current form while releasing them from conservatorship. One group has funded a proposal that would provide incentives for the institutions to issue risk transfers without mandating them.

Risk-transfers are a useful tool, said Landon Parsons, a senior adviser at Moelis & Company, which created the plan, but they "may not be available during a business cycle downturn at a reasonable cost."

Some of the securities' strongest advocates include those who want the companies to be dismantled, replaced with a larger number of private firms or a single, government-run utility, which they say would disperse risk and avoid another taxpayer-funded bailout.

Many shareholders, however, want to preserve the companies in near their current form while releasing them from conservatorship. One group has funded a proposal that would provide incentives for the institutions to issue risk transfers without mandating them.

Risk-transfers are a useful tool, said Landon Parsons, a senior adviser at Moelis & Company, which created the plan, but they "may not be available during a business cycle downturn at a reasonable cost."

Fund managers deny AI threatens jobs

Madison Marriage 
1,303 words
14 August 2017
04:05
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A prestigious Boston-based consultancy issued a "http://www.opimas.com/research/210/detail/" to the asset management industry this year: within the next seven years, more than 90,000 jobs in the sector would be eliminated by the rise of artificial intelligence.

Opimas, the company behind the prediction, said asset management would be worse affected than other areas of financial services, which is likely to lose 230,000 jobs overall by 2025. The consultancy estimates about 520,000 people work in the asset management industry globally.

Octavio Marenzi, chief executive of Opimas, says AI will lead to job cuts in the back office, operations and customer service.  Some portfolio managers feel that AI cannot replace them in the investment-decision process. However, we are already seeing this replacement occur,  he says.  This is particularly true with a shift to passive investment strategies, where a surprising number of investment decisions are still handled by human portfolio managers. This is an area ready to be automated by AI. 

However, fund companies fiercely deny that their growing use of machine-learning techniques, which help portfolio managers spot patterns in vast streams of data, will trigger a dramatic fall in headcount.

Although machine-learning algorithms have been used by computer-driven hedge funds for decades, Baillie Gifford is one of several mainstream fund managers that are turning to AI for the first time in a bid to boost returns.

Kyle McEnery, an investment analyst at the Scottish fund house, says his colleagues  had a long talk about jobs being under threat  when the project was in its early stages.

 The first very positive thing I would say is anything that we automate will only mean investors have more time doing much more valuable tasks and coming up with new ideas,  he says.  In investment in particular, I think this would be mainly welcomed by our human investors. 

Baillie Gifford, one of the UK s oldest asset managers, has hired a mathematician to help with its AI project, which is being led by four staff from its IT and investment teams. The Edinburgh-based company will hire more specialists if it becomes  really obvious  the algorithms are improving investment performance, Mr McEnery adds.

A senior executive at a large hedge fund, speaking privately, says fears that widespread adoption of AI could result in mass redundancies for high-earning portfolio managers are misplaced.

 If you are executing small trades, I think most people would agree a machine is better at doing that. If you are trying to understand the prospects of a company by speaking to a new chief executive, at the moment, everyone would say a human is better at doing that,  he says.

 It is about understanding what machines are better at. There are certain jobs that have been automated over the past 10 years. But I don t think the whole of finance will be automated away. AI removes some work, but creates different work. 

Deutsche Asset Management, Germany s largest investment house, has also started investigating how to use AI to improve sales techniques and attract clients. The  ¬711bn asset manager has exchanged data sets with several technology companies that specialise in AI analysis and will soon decide which provider to work with.

The Frankfurt-based company wants to use AI to introduce chatbots that can respond to simple questions from retail clients when they come to its website. It also wants to automate how it evaluates meeting reports filed by its thousands of sales staff to get a better indication of mass changes in client sentiment.

Deutsche AM additionally wants to sift through publicly available data on the performance of clients  investments with rival asset managers. This could, in theory, help it judge when best to approach clients about switching their money into better-performing funds.

Thorsten Michalik, head of distribution in Europe and Asia for Deutsche AM, denies that using machine learning to streamline these processes will hit headcount in his division.  From the information generated by AI, we hope we will increase the size of our business, which will mean we need to hire more people,  he says.

In Germany, only 15 per cent of the population invests in funds and stocks, he says. He believes AI will help Deutsche AM access the 70m Germans who have never invested, bringing more money into the company and increasing the need for more staff. Mr Michalik s team has three specialists focused on AI, and he expects this will triple over the next 12 months.

 We would really like to reach, via AI, the 85 per cent who are not currently invested. We need to increase the size of the market. The only way we can do this is by using new technology. [Our] people understand this,  he says.

The huge interest in AI has created jobs elsewhere across the sector. According to LinkedIn, the professional networking website, there has been a surge in the number of financial services companies, including banks and asset managers, seeking data scientists over the past two years.

There are 6,300 data scientist jobs within financial services currently advertised on LinkedIn.

The number of roles advertised in the sector using the terms data scientist, machine learning or artificial intelligence, has more than tripled over the past two years.

Steve Cohen s Point72, Neuberger Berman and Wellington Capital are among the large alternative investment managers that are looking to hire more data scientists to work alongside their investment teams.

Investors  growing interest in this area comes on the back of an explosion in the amount of data available online.

According to Unigestion, the Swiss asset manager, 90 per cent of available data has been created in the past three years, coming from a mix of sources, including weather sensors, social networks, satellite images, online transactions and GPS signals.

With the internet of things   everyday objects connected to the internet   forecast to grow from 8bn to 20bn devices by 2020, the number of data points will grow exponentially, the Geneva-based asset manager said in a recent research paper.

Other mainstream asset managers are also entering the fray. State Street Global Exchange, a division of State Street, the US custody bank and asset manager, is piloting a machine-learning tool that aims to send alerts to large investors when news breaks that could hurt their portfolios. If a copper mine explodes in North America, for example, State Street could alert clients with large holdings in Apple to review their positions in the technology company, as it relies on the metal to make its iPhones.

Japan s Nomura Asset Management, "https://www.nri.com/global/news/2017/170626_1.aspx" in June it had completed a project seeking to assess whether AI can improve the decision-making processes of its portfolio managers. The project, which was carried out with the Nomura Research Institute, a consultancy, involved systematically labelling data sources for fund managers, such as Twitter feeds, blogs or news announcements, as positive or negative.

This helped Nomura s investment teams to become  more efficient  and freed up their time to investigate more interesting investment ideas, according to a spokesman for the NRI.

Aldous Birchall, AI lead in the consulting business of PwC, the professional services firm, is more circumspect. He believes some portfolio manager roles will be eliminated as AI is adopted by traditional fund managers.

But he adds:  I would be really surprised to see an apocalypse. I don t think people will come into the office and be told they have been replaced by a robot. Rather than a team of 10 [portfolio managers running a fund], it will be a team of seven or eight, including a couple of data scientists.

 I often get asked   what s better, AI or human fund managers? The answer is the best team involves humans plus AI.

Baillie Gifford plans to use AI to improve fund performance

Madison Marriage 
576 words
14 August 2017
04:14
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Baillie Gifford has launched a project exploring how to harness artificial intelligence to improve the performance of its funds, becoming one of the first traditional investment companies to push into an area traditionally dominated by hedge funds.

The 109-year-old Scottish asset manager embarked on its AI mission six months ago, following in the footsteps of big hedge fund companies such as Man Group and Winton Capital, which have used machine-learning techniques for years.

Baillie Gifford has hired a former mathematician to assist with the project, and has four internal staff from its IT and investment teams focused on the initiative. The goal is to assess whether AI can eliminate mundane tasks that take up fund managers  time, enabling them to investigate a wider range of potentially market-beating investment ideas.

The Edinburgh-based fund house, which tends to hold stocks for a minimum of five years, plans to assess whether AI has a discernible impact on fund performance before hiring more specialists in this area.

Despite its reputation as a traditional stockpicking asset manager, Baillie Gifford has been a sizeable and often early-stage investor in several cutting-edge technology companies, including Chinese conglomerates Alibaba, Baidu and Tencent; Facebook, the social networking site; and Amazon, the online retailer.

Kyle McEnery, an investment analyst at the fund house and a former physicist who is closely involved in the project, said the company felt compelled to explore the potential benefits of AI, given the  explosion of data and the increase in processing power  in recent years.

 Considering we always look at things in the long term, understanding where things could go is something we should be doing,  he said.  Our approach is helping our investors to do what they do better. We are breaking down the investment process and seeing if there are any areas where a computer would be helpful   if a human is wasting their time on an area when it would be better spent on a higher value task. 

The fund house, which oversees 145bn of assets, is testing algorithms that can scan for stocks that might suit a particular investment style. The global equity team might want to search for companies that generate a certain level of sales revenue or gross profits, have significant insider ownership and meet several other criteria.

 The power of machine learning is you can put in much more than five criteria, and allow [a computer] the freedom to place different weights on the data you put in. [The algorithm could then] supply that team with a constant stream of research ideas, giving them much more competition in their process, which leads to deeper due diligence and debate,  Mr McEnery said.

The asset management arms of several banks and insurers, including BNP Paribas and Deutsche Bank, have also been ahead of the curve in terms of exploring how AI could benefit their investment and sales teams.

A growing number of mainstream asset managers are looking to adopt AI to shore up their performance, according to Aldous Birchall, AI lead at PwC, the professional services firm.

 Specialised quant funds have been using this for a long time. What we are seeing now is a broader acceptance [of AI] across the traditional asset management industry,  he said.  It will enable them to do a lot more with the same resources: cover more companies and assess more opportunities.

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT
Fund managers deny AI threatens jobs; INVESTMENT STRATEGY

By Madison Marriage 
1,277 words
14 August 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FNM1
10
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Industry believes artificial intelligence will actually create more opportunities

A prestigious Boston-based consultancy issued a warning to the asset management industry this year: within the next seven years, more than 90,000 jobs in the sector would be eliminated by the rise of artificial intelligence.

Opimas, the company behind the prediction, said asset management would be worse affected than other areas of financial services, which is likely to lose 230,000 jobs overall by 2025. The consultancy estimates about 520,000 people work in the asset management industry globally.

Octavio Marenzi, chief executive of Opimas, says AI will lead to job cuts in the back office, operations and customer service. "Some portfolio managers feel that AI cannot replace them in the investment-decision process. However, we are already seeing this replacement occur," he says. "This is particularly true with a shift to passive investment strategies, where a surprising number of investment decisions are still handled by human portfolio managers. This is an area ready to be automated by AI."

However, fund companies fiercely deny that their growing use of machine-learning techniques, which help portfolio managers spot patterns in vast streams of data, will trigger a dramatic fall in headcount.

Although machine-learning algorithms have been used by computer-driven hedge funds for decades, Baillie Gifford is one of several mainstream fund managers that are turning to AI for the first time in a bid to boost returns.

Kyle McEnery, an investment analyst at the Scottish fund house, says his colleagues "had a long talk about jobs being under threat " when the project was in its early stages.

"The first very positive thing I would say is anything that we automate will only mean investors have more time doing much more valuable tasks and coming up with new ideas," he says.

Baillie Gifford has hired a mathematician to help with its AI project, which is being led by four staff from its IT and investment teams. The Edinburgh-based company will hire more specialists if it becomes "really obvious" the algorithms are improving investment performance, Mr McEnery adds.

A senior executive at a large hedge fund, speaking privately, says fears that widespread adoption of AI could result in mass redundancies for high-earning portfolio managers are misplaced.

"If you are executing small trades, I think most people would agree a machine is better at doing that. If you are trying to understand the prospects of a company by speaking to a new chief executive, at the moment, everyone would say a human is better at doing that," he says.

"It is about understanding what machines are better at. There are certain jobs that have been automated over the past 10 years. But I don't think the whole of finance will be automated away. AI removes some work, but creates different work."

Deutsche Asset Management, Germany's second-largest investment house, has also started investigating how to use AI to improve sales techniques and attract clients. The  ¬711bn asset manager has exchanged data sets with several technology companies that specialise in AI analysis and will soon decide which provider to work with.

The Frankfurt-based company wants to use AI to introduce chatbots that can respond to simple questions from retail clients when they come to its website. It also wants to automate how it evaluates meeting reports filed by its thousands of sales staff to get a better indication of mass changes in client sentiment.

Deutsche AM additionally wants to sift through publicly available data on the performance of clients' investments with rival asset managers. This could, in theory, help it judge when best to approach clients about switching their money into better-performing funds.

Thorsten Michalik, head of distribution in Europe and Asia for Deutsche AM, denies that using machine learning to streamline these processes will hit headcount. "From the information generated by AI, we hope we will increase the size of our business, which will mean we need to hire more people," he says.

In Germany, only 15 per cent of the population invests in funds and stocks, he says. He believes AI will help Deutsche AM access the 70m Germans who have never invested, bringing more money into the company and increasing the need for more staff. Mr Michalik's team has three specialists focused on AI, and he expects this will triple over the next 12 months.

"We need to increase the size of the market. The only way we can do this is by using new technology. [Our] people understand this," he says.

The huge interest in AI has created jobs elsewhere across the sector. According to LinkedIn, the professional networking website, there has been a surge in the number of financial services companies, including banks and asset managers, seeking data scientists over the past two years.

There are 6,300 data scientist jobs within financial services currently advertised on LinkedIn.

The number of roles advertised in the sector using the terms data scientist, machine learning or artificial intelligence, has more than tripled over the past two years.

Steve Cohen's Point72, Neuberger Berman and Wellington Capital are among the large alternative investment managers that are looking to hire more data scientists to work alongside their investment teams.

Investors' growing interest in this area comes on the back of an explosion in the amount of data available online.

According to Unigestion, the Swiss asset manager, 90 per cent of available data has been created in the past three years, coming from a mix of sources, including weather sensors, social networks, satellite images, online transactions and GPS signals.

With the internet of things - everyday objects connected to the internet - forecast to grow from 8bn to 20bn devices by 2020, the number of data points will grow exponentially, the Geneva-based asset manager said in a recent research paper.

Other mainstream asset managers are also entering the fray. State Street Global Exchange, a division of State Street, the US custody bank and asset manager, is piloting a machine-learning tool that aims to send alerts to large investors when news breaks that could hurt their portfolios. If a copper mine explodes in North America, for example, State Street could alert clients with large holdings in Apple to review their positions in the technology company, as it relies on the metal to make its iPhones.

Japan's Nomura Asset Management, said in June it had completed a project seeking to assess whether AI can improve the decision-making processes of its portfolio managers. The project, which was carried out with the Nomura Research Institute, a consultancy, involved systematically labelling data sources for fund managers, such as Twitter feeds, blogs or news announcements, as positive or negative.

This helped Nomura's investment teams to become "more efficient" and freed up their time to investigate more interesting investment ideas, according to a spokesman for the NRI.

Aldous Birchall, AI lead in the consulting business of PwC, the professional services firm, is more circumspect. He believes some portfolio manager roles will be eliminated as AI is adopted by traditional fund managers.

But he adds: "I would be really surprised to see an apocalypse. I don't think people will come into the office and be told they have been replaced by a robot. Rather than a team of 10 [portfolio managers running a fund], it will be a team of seven or eight, including a couple of data scientists.

"I often get asked - what's better, AI or human fund managers? The answer is the best team involves humans plus AI."

Business
WebMD Wants to Go Beyond Medical Information; The site is aiming to be a place where health consumers can also take action

By Ali Stratton 
1,563 words
14 August 2017
03:12
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

In the digital age, people seeking health-related assistance online have more options than ever before. Innovative technologies, as well as cloud computing and machine learning enable the newly medicalized smartphone to permeate every aspect of health care.

As a health information provider, WebMD is well aware of these changes. According to the company's chief financial officer, Blake DeSimone, who was appointed to the position in September, WebMD is in the process of transforming its flagship site for consumers, WebMD.com, from a one-stop shop for information to a platform people can engage with and take action to improve their health.

WebMD last month agreed to be acquired by private-equity firm KKR & Co. for $2.8 billion, after publicly putting itself in play early this year. When announcing the deal, WebMD said the acquisition would provide flexibility and bolster its resources.

Mr. DeSimone, 40, joined WebMD in 2015 and previously served as senior vice president of finance until his appointment to the executive position.

In an interview, Mr. DeSimone, while declining to talk about the pending acquisition, spoke about transparency, expansion to new interfaces, and how WebMD is leveraging its formidable customer base to make advancements in the digital age. Edited excerpts follow:

WSJ: According to WebMD, 75 million people turn to it each month for medical information. How does WebMD ensure that the advice it gives is accurate and people can trust it?

MR. DESIMONE: It really manifests itself in two ways. One, being independent. On Medscape and WebMD we have complete separation between our editorial teams and our commercial teams internally so advertisers will never have an influence on the content created by the editorial teams. The second priority there is transparency. We are a publisher, so there are places on the sites where there is advertiser-sponsored content, and to the extent that there is that content it is labeled as such.

WSJ: Let's talk about how technology is transforming health care in the digital age. People can now reach doctors through their phones and get instant medical advice. Is that WebMD's competition?

MR. DESIMONE: Over the course of the last 20 years WebMD has been a place where consumers and patients and caregivers go to get content, but once they get that content they have to go somewhere else to take the next step in managing their health. We're working to transition the platform to be more than just a place where you get information to a place where you take action.

We're in the middle of that evolution right now. No one has the scale that we have right now. So it's up to us to transition the asset to provide these other services so that we can improve the engagement with our audiences.

WSJ: Can you list specific changes WebMD is making to enable its customers to take action?

MR. DESIMONE: We can build upon products like our symptom checkers and provide our customers a variety of services like booking a doctor, ordering a lab test or having a telemedicine visit.

If a customer was searching for information on a certain condition and then read something on WebMD that motivated them to want to speak with a doctor, in the future, we'll provide that person with the option of scheduling an appointment or engaging in a telemedicine visit. There could be a link on the page, or a window that would come up, but it would be available to them right on the page they're reading. As you go through the symptom checker experience, the same options would be available.

WSJ: Could WebMD's transition from an information source to a place to take action create revenue-producing opportunities for the company?

MR. DESIMONE: Yes. Currently, all of the revenue that we generate on WebMD and Medscape are advertising and sponsorship based. As we move to areas where consumers can take action, we do believe that presents a new transactional revenue stream to the company.

WSJ: Does WebMD believe insurance will cover the expenses patients will incur by using the platform to take action?

MR. DESIMONE: We plan to help them understand potential costs. And that includes whether or not it's covered by insurance. We will provide customers with billing information that they can submit to their insurance company for reimbursement. In the future we will explore relationships with providers to make a seamless and transparent process for consumers.

WSJ: Some doctors could see WebMD inserting itself between them and their patients, in its efforts to help customers to take action. What do you think about that?

MR. DESIMONE: Seventy percent of customers who visit WebMD for information about a disease or condition actually make an appointment to see their doctor within 30 days. If we can make it easier for customers to make an appointment with a doctor or schedule a telemedicine visit, it's good for consumers and for doctors. We're not looking to interrupt that relationship at all. We're just giving them additional options. Ultimately, it's up to a consumer to make the decision that's right for them.

WSJ: With the opportunity to point people to specific products or services, how will WebMD ensure that its customers understand they aren't getting impartial advice?

MR. DESIMONE: Trust with our audience is something that WebMD has been known for, so we need to maintain that trust. Our editorial team is separate from our commercial teams. All the information we provide is independent. We expect to continue to be independent and not necessarily endorse one telemedicine vendor or lab-test vendor versus another. In cases where we did do that, we would be completely transparent and identify that as being branded by a specific vendor.

WSJ: Tell me about some of the new platforms WebMD is expanding to, from its traditional website.

MR. DESIMONE: According to Gartner, 30% of web browsing sessions will be done without a screen, through alternative interfaces by 2020. We are doing that through Amazon Alexa and Google Home and Facebook messenger. WebMD will respond with physician-reviewed answers, and basically all of the information we currently have on the site will be available through these platforms. We want to make our content available to all audiences wherever they are to get access to health-related information.

WSJ: How is WebMD using big data?

MR. DESIMONE: Over the last couple of years we've made significant investments in research and analytics resources and teams so we can improve the WebMD experience.

A good example of how we've leveraged data for customers is the work we've done with Reckitt Benckiser for cold and flu. WebMD now publishes interactive cold and flu maps annually to present real-time analysis of the spread of cold and flu. We can now predict cold and flu trends up to four weeks in advance. It leverages various data sources we have including those from WebMD's symptom checker. The data we collect informs [Reckitt Benckiser's] media and marketing plans and it's really invaluable to them.

WSJ: How does Reckitt Benckiser use this information to its advantage?

MR. DESIMONE: WebMD's proprietary models in the cold and flu map are able to predict cold and flu trends in advance. Reckitt Benckiser then uses those insights to target specific products like Mucinex for users with the symptoms that are most prevalent in a given geography. If nasal congestion is at a high level in New York City, Reckitt Benckiser might trigger an ad for their Mucinex Sinus Max. Or, if cough and chest congestion is prevalent in San Francisco, they might trigger an ad for Mucinex Expectorant. They're all over-the-counter products. And the campaign is conducted on a completely de-identified basis and the ads they deliver are based solely on geography, so it's not targeting an individual.

WSJ: WebMD has a reputation of scaring people who turn to it for medical questions. What is the company doing to overcome that?

MR. DESIMONE: Currently we correlate visitors' symptoms with possible medical conditions based in part on the prevalence rate. The new version of our symptom checker that's launching this summer does two things. It's easier to enter symptoms and it will leverage an even larger library of symptoms and possible conditions.

It uses a "match strength" rating system of weak, fair, moderate or strong, to help audiences determine the likelihood of a given condition and give perspective for what the right next step is.

Ms. Stratton is a Wall Street Journal reporter in New York. She can be reached at "mailto:ali.stratton@wsj.com".

Pro Private Markets
Midmarket Firms Raise Bets on Technology

By Jessica Davies 
605 words
14 August 2017
21:00
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Two private-equity firms are returning to the marketplace with new technology funds that dwarf the scale of their previous vehicles, as capital amassed for information technology strategies is swelling.

Zouk Capital an investor in Swedish contactless card machine maker iZettle AB is raising its third growth capital fund in the low-carbon tech space, according to people familiar with the matter. The London firm will target  ¬300 million ($353.5 million) for the vehicle nearly as much as the  ¬370 million it has raised to date, according to its website.

Double the Size

C5 Capital, another London firm that invests both private equity and venture capital in data analytics and cybersecurity companies, plans to return to market with a third fund before the end of 2017, according to people familiar with the matter. The firm is expected to target approximately $200 million for the vehicle, nearly double the size of its first fund.

Meanwhile, the firm will also start raising capital for a $150 million vehicle to invest in cloud computing companies and software sellers, imminently, a separate person said. In 2015, the firm partnered with Amazon Web Services for a cloud-based accelerator program.

These fundraising plans come as appetite for technology investment has soared in recent months pushing some price multiples to above 20 times earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortisation and coincide with a surge in fundraising for information technology-focused strategies.

HgCapital said in February it had closed a 2.5 billion core fundraising effort alongside its 575 million HgCapital Mercury II fund, both of which invest in technology and technology-enabled services. Vitruvian Partners, which has invested in travel fare aggregator Skyscanner Ltd. and software asset management technology provider Snow Software, said in June it had raised about  ¬2.4 billion, hitting its hard cap.

Recently established firm Volpi Capital, which was set up by 3i Group PLC's former head of technology, media and telecommunications Crevan O'Grady, began talks with investors late last year to raise a  ¬300 million fund to invest in European businesses.

"There's growth because they're taking market share," said Adam Turtle, a partner at private-equity advisory Rede Partners LLP. He added that health-care and tech "would fit under the defensive tech segment", insulating them from economic cycles.

Software is eating the world, the saying goes, and private-equity firms are investing in software-as-a-service companies structured around cloud-based services with aplomb.

Congested Space

Andrew Ferguson, managing director at Baird Capital Partners Europe, described private equity particularly in the lower to midmarket, as "a congested space".

His firm invests in technology only "where it powers service companies", such as IT service providers, rather than investing in technology manufacturers.

Research provider Preqin Ltd. said in its 2017 Global Private Equity and Venture Capital report that funds focused on the IT sector were targeting among "the largest sums of capital" of all funds in the asset class, amounting to a total of $29 billion in aggregate as of April.

Strong appetite to invest in the sector comes as the line between private equity and venture capital is blurring. Private-equity firms are investing growth capital in revenue-generating companies and venture-capital funds are getting larger.

Appetite for investment in European technology was kickstarted when Skype Technologies SA, one of the region's first unicorns, filed to list on the public markets in 2010 and sold to Microsoft Corp. for $8.5 billion a year later.
